Preface 
 
This master’s thesis is the result of a 30 ECTS point project, which was carried out under the supervision of 
Doctor Katrine Qvortrup, in the period from end of January to the end of June 2015. The experimental work 
was carried out from the Department of Chemistry at the Technical University of Denmark.  
This report is divided into two main sections. The first part covers the theoretical background and related 
relevant literature reports for HDAC substrates and inhibitors, solid phase peptide synthesis, and bead based 
screening technologies. The experimental results obtained from this project are discussed in the second part.   
Working on this project has been an enjoyable and educational experience, which has enabled me to work 
in a multidisciplinary field, exploiting both solid- and solution-phase organic synthesis, combinatorial 
chemistry, and molecular biology. For this, I would first and foremost like to thank my supervisor Katrine 
Qvortrup, for enabling me to work on such an exciting project. Her encouragement and eagerness to discuss 
results and new ideas have been very inspiring. The competences I have obtained from working under 
Katrines supervision has expanded my knowledge in the field of organic chemistry and for that, I am truly 
thankful.  
Among all the members of the group who have made this project an enjoyable experience, I would like to 
give a special thanks to Remi Mikkelsen for his assistance in the laboratory. For technical assistance, I would 
like to thank Tina Gustafsson, Anne E. Hector, and Brian Ekman-Gregersen. 
I would like to thank the Novo Scholarship Programme for financial support during the project period, which 
has enabled me to put my full focus on the project. 
Finally, I would like to express my gratitude to my friends, family, and girlfriend Kia, for great support and 
encouragement throughout this project and during my education as a soon to be M.Sc. Eng. Chem. 
It is my hope that the experimental work that has been carried out in this project will establish a good 
foundation for future development and thus expand our knowledge of the essential histone deacetylase 
enzymes.

vi 
 
 
 
==PAGE:9==
vii 
 
Abstract  
 
The histone deacetylase enzymes have been identified as attractive targets for both cancerous and non-
cancerous diseases.  Inhibitors of these enzymes have been a topic of interest for the pharmaceutical industry 
for more than two decades, and currently a few of these inhibitors have been approved for treatment of T-
cell lymphoma. However, clinical studies of these pan-selective inhibitors has revealed toxicity profiles, 
including gastrointestinal disturbances, fatigue, and QT prolongation. This knowledge has stimulated the 
interest for developing new isoform selective inhibitors, to reduce the toxic side effects associated with the 
current treatment, and targeting more specific diseases. 
The desire for identifying a full chemical profiling of these histone deacetylase subtypes has in this project 
been addressed by creating the foundation for a new high throughput screening format, to identify selective 
and highly active substrates for these enzymes. This screening format is an adaption of the newly invented 
in-bead screening technology, which rely on the physical prospect that a small organic compound preferable 
stay localized inside the organic environment of a polymeric resin, whenever the surrounding media 
constitute an aqueous buffer. The substrates are liberated from a solid support in a biocompatible 
environment through photolytic release.  
The linker construct that are capable of cleaving these active substrates was created by anchoring the ε-N of 
a Boc-Lys-AMC residue to Holmes photolinker. This unique linker design enabled selective modifications on 
both the α-N and ε-N of the lysine moiety, thus creating ideal conditions for a fast and efficient library 
development. Initially, the platform for the library was established by a pre solution-based assay screen of 
active substrates with more than 14 different ε-N modifications, which was synthesized from the previous 
developed linker construct on solid support. Upon identification of the desired ε-N modification a one-bead-
one-compound library of more than 63,000 different compounds was developed. This was followed by 
careful optimization of the in-bead assay screen, which verified that this method can be utilized to identify 
active substrates based on their relative fluorescence intensity within the individual bead.  
 
==PAGE:10==
viii 
 
 
 
==PAGE:11==
ix 
 
Resumé 
 
Histon deacetyltransferase enzymerne er blevet identificeret som værende attraktive målgrupper for både 
carcinogene og ikke-carcinogene sygdomme. Inhibitorer for disse enzymer har vist sig attraktive for den 
farmaceutiske industri i mere end to årtier. Der findes i dag lægemidler på markedet, der fungerer som 
inhibitorer for disse enzymer og som er blevet godkendt til behandling af T-celle lymfekræft. Behandling med 
disse pan-selektive inhibitorer har dog vist, at medføre bivirkninger såsom gastrointestinale forstyrrelser, 
udmattelse, samt QT-forlængelser. Denne viden har stimuleret interessen for udvikling af nye og selektive 
inhibitorer, for at mindske de toksiske bivirkninger, samt målrette behandling af mere specifikke sygsomme.  
En fuld profilanalyse af disse HDAC subtyper er meget attraktiv. Dette projekt har fokuseret på at udvikle et 
fundament for et nyt ”high throughput screening” format, for at identificere nye, aktive og selektive 
substrater for disse enzymer. Dette format bygger på den nyligt udviklede in-bead screening teknologi. ”In-
bead” henfører til muliggøresen, at kunne screene små organiske stoffer inde i en polymerkugle, som følge 
af en fotokløvning af disse stoffer fra polymerkuglen, når denne befinder sig i et vandigt miljø.  
En linker opbygning som muliggøre frigørelsen af aktive stoffer via fotokløvning var udviklet, ved at koble den 
funktionaliserede aminosyre Boc-Lys-AMC med Holmes fotolinker. Dette unikke linker-design muliggjorde at 
lysin derivatet kunne funktionaliseres fra både dets α-N og ε-N selektivt, hvilket etablerede ideelle 
betingelser for dannelse af et bibliotek af stoffer.  
Substrater med 14 forskellige modifikationer på lysinets ε-N var syntetiseret via fast fase kemi og aktiviteten 
af disse stoffer blev testet overfor alle 11 Zink-afhængige subtyper. Dette gav grundlag for udvikling af et 
bibliotek med mere en 63.000 forskellige stoffer, i hvad der er kendt som et ”one-bead-one-compound” 
bibliotek.  Heraf efterfulgt var optimeringen af dette in-bead assay, hvorfra det blev verificeret, at denne 
metode kunne bruges til at identificere aktive substrater i polymerkuglerne, baseret på deres relative 
fluorescens intensitet.

1 Introduction 
 
In medicinal chemistry, knowledge about the reactivity and specificity of a target enzyme is crucial for 
developing new and potent inhibitors. The regulating mechanisms in DNA transcription have been an area of 
great interest, thus controlling the expression of genes within the cell. The transcription of DNA in cells are 
an essential role for the life and development of the individual cell. In resting eukaryotic cells, the DNA is 
tightly packed to protect the double helix, and to prevent the accessibility of transcription factors [1]. DNA is 
packed into chromatin, a highly organized and dynamic protein-DNA complex. The fundamental subunit of 
chromatin is the nucleosome. The nucleosome is surrounded by 164 base pairs of DNA, and is an octamer 
consisting of two copies of each of the core histones; H2A (dimer), H2B (dimer), H3 (tetramer), and H4 
(tetramer). Activation of gene transcription in otherwise inaccessible DNA regions is depending on the post-
translational modification (PTM) of the histones N-amino tails i.e. the ε-N lysine acetylations (see Figure 1). 
Positions for the most important acetylations on the histones have been proposed as being the Lys9 and Lys14 
on histone H3, and Lys5, Lys8, Lys12, and Lys16 on histone H4 [2]. Lysine residues are positively charged at 
physiological pH and are highly attracted by electrostatic interactions of the negatively charged phosphate 
backbone of DNA [3].  
Upon acetylation, the positive charge of the lysine residues ε-N are removed, thus transforming the 
chromatin into a more relaxed structure, which is associated with a higher degree of gene transcription [4]. 
The balance of the steady-state levels of acetylation is primarily controlled by the opposite actions and 
activity of histone acetyltransferases (HATs) and histone deacetylases (HDACs). Hyperacetylation are 
Figure 1 Schematic illustration of the effect of lysine ε-N acetylation on the
histone tails, and the accessibility of the DNA strain. Acetylations 
caused by HAT create a more relaxed structure, which is associated with 
a higher degree of gene transcription.  
==PAGE:16==
2 
 
increased levels of histone acetylation and are associated with increased transcriptional activity, whereas 
hypoacetylation is decreased levels of acetylation, leading to repression of gene expression.   
The inhibition of HDAC enzymes leads to hyperacetylation, and potential inhibitors for these deacetylases 
have gained great interest in the pharmaceutical industry for treatment of both cancerous [5], and 
noncancerous types of diseases, such as neurodegenerative disorder [6], chronic pain [7], cystic fibrosis [8], 
diabetes [9], and affects on learning and memory [10].  
This project seeks to develop a new platform for a bead based high throughput technology for identification 
of novel and highly active substrates for these HDAC enzymes, thus expanding our knowledge towards the 
individual subtype. In the following, the relevant theoretical background will be presented prior to the 
discussion of the results that have been obtained during this project.  
 
1.1 Histone deacetylases 
In humans, 18 different hydrolytic enzymes originally known as histone deacetylases (HDACs), have been 
identified and classified according to their sequence similarity and homology with their yeast counterparts. 
Eleven of these enzymes are zinc-dependent and are divided into the following classes; class I (HDAC1, 2, 3, 
and 8), class IIa (HDAC4, 5, 7, and 9), class IIb (HDAC6, and HDAC10), and class IV (HDAC11) [11]. The 
remaining seven enzymes are divided into class III, and are a family of NAD+-dependent deacetylases, known 
as sirtuins (silent information regulator 
proteins) (SIRT1-7) [12]. The sirtuins do not 
only 
differ 
from 
the 
Zinc-dependent 
isoforms mechanistically, but also towards 
their specific activity. SIRT4, 6 and 7 have 
shown to have little to no deacetylase 
activity, but members of the family have 
however displayed great activity towards 
several 
other 
deacylaltions, 
such 
as; 
succinylation (SIRT5), malonylation (SIRT5), 
crotonylation (SIRT1 and 2 (weak effect)), 
and myristoylation (SIRT6) [13] (For lysine 
PTMs see Figure 6). SIRT4 has furthermore 
shown to facilitate ADP ribosylation. The 
Figure 2 Location of the different HDAC isoforms within the human 
body. 
==PAGE:17==
3 
 
activity of sirtuins has gained great interest lately, due to their specificity towards other acylated lysine 
derivatives, thus regulating yet undiscovered PTMs. Furthermore, the enzymes have been linked to a variety 
of phenotypes and are considered targets for therapeutic intervention in a broad spectrum of diseases, 
including various cancers, diabetes, cardiovascular disease, and neurodegeneration [14]. However, these 
NAD+-dependent deacetylases are beyond the scope of this project and will not be further discussed.  
The different HDAC isoforms are located differently within the cell, divided into their respective class. The 
class I HDACs are ubiquitously expressed, and are normally found in the nucleus, whereas the class II and IV 
are restricted to certain tissues such as; heart (HDAC4, 5, 7, 6, and 11), skeletal muscles (HDAC4, 5, 7, 9, and 
11), brain (HDAC4, 5, 9, and 11), placenta (HDAC7), pancreas (HDAC7, and 6), liver (HDAC6, and 10), kidney 
(HDAC6, 10, and 11), and spleen (HDAC10) and are found both in the nucleus and the cytoplasm [15] (see 
Figure 2).  
Extensive studies regarding the mechanism of the HDAC enzymes have been conducted and proposed 
mechanisms are primarily based on X-ray crystal structures of HDAC8 or histone deactylase like proteins 
Figure 3  Proposed mechanism of lysine ε-N acetylated substrate hydrolysis in HDAC8, adapted from Dowling et al. [19]. 
==PAGE:18==
4 
 
complexed with known inhibitors [16, 17, 18]. Competitive inhibitors such as TSA and SAHA (see Figure 7 and 
Figure 8) contain hydroxamic acids, which chelates to the Zn2+ directly in the active site. These structural 
studies have led to a proposed metal-dependent histone deacetylase mechanism, as shown in Figure 3 [19]. 
The Zn2+ binds water within hydrogen-bonding distance to both histidine H142 and H143 (A). Upon insertion 
of the acetylated lysine substrate, the acetyl carbonyl is polarized by coordination to both Zn2+ and hydrogen-
bonding with tyrosine Y306 (B). A nucleophilic attack from the metal-bound water creates a tetrahedral 
intermediate, whose oxyanion is stabilized by Y306 (C). A proton transfer from H143 to the formed lysine 
amino group facilitates the collapse of the formed intermediate, thus releasing the deacetylated lysine and 
acetic acid (D).  
 
1.2 HDAC inhibitors for treatment of cancer 
Cancer remains one of the most feared diseases in the modern world and according to the World Health 
Organization, it is responsible for a quarter of all deaths in the developed world during the year 2000. Cancer 
cells are formed when normal cells lose their regulatory mechanism that control growth and multiplication. 
A major problem in treating cancer, is that it is not a single disease and more than 200 cancers resulted from 
different effects are known [20]. There are three traditional ways of treating cancer, which is surgery, 
radiotherapy, and chemotherapy. When performing chemotherapy, which include treatment with HDAC 
inhibitors (HDACI), a major issue is that cancer cells are derived from normal cells and treatments with anti-
cancer drugs often target both cell types, hence severe toxicity profiles and side effects are associated with 
these treatments. Since cancer cells often grow faster than normal cells, they often have a tendency to 
accumulate nutrients, synthetic building blocks, and drugs more quickly than normal cells. This cancer cell 
phenotype has given rise to some selectivity when using general chemotherapeutic drugs, but there are also 
normal cells in the human body that grow rapidly, such as bone marrow cells, blood cells, hair cells, and skin 
cells. Therefore, effectivity and selectivity are crucial factors when developing new anti-cancer drugs and a 
better understanding of the molecular mechanisms behind the specific cancers can help the design of these 
drugs.  
One specific tendency observed in many cancer types is the alteration of the expression of the individual 
HDAC subtypes. For a cell to maintain normal cell growth, a good balance between acetylation and 
deacetylation is essential. Overexpression of HDACs in normal cells can lead to decreasing levels of histone 
acetylation and local alterations in the structure of chromatin. This facilitates gene-specific repression of 
transcription and is presumably influenced by other histone modifications and promoters [21]. The repressed 
==PAGE:19==
5 
 
genes are often tumor suppressors, cell-cycle inhibitors, and differentiation factors, or apoptosis inducers. 
Loss of expression in any of these classes of proteins would be advantageous for a cancerous cell. Since these 
phenotypes are an indirect effect of the altered activity of the HDAC enzymes, inhibitions of these enzymes 
are proposed to alter the progression of these cells, thus leading to apoptosis. In the following, some 
examples of HDACs effect on cancer will be discussed. 
 
1.2.1 Abnormalities in cell cycle regulation 
The normal growth of a cell is a tightly controlled and regulated process. The cell undergoes four phases 
known as G1, S, G2, and M within one cycle, and for the cell to pass from one stage to another is dependent 
on a series of signals (see Figure 4). For a cell to pass a stage, it must ensure that the DNA is not damaged or 
mutated, that all chromosomes are intact, and that it has enough nutrients. If these criteria are not met, the 
cell can either choose to stay at that specific state or commit suicide (apoptosis). The control of these cell 
cycles involves a variety of proteins called cyclins, and cyclin dependent kinases (CDKs). Increasing 
concentrations of these cyclins and CDKs is a signal for the cell, to move to the next phase. To ensure a normal 
growth, the activity of these CDKs are regulated by cyclin-dependent kinase inhibitors. One of such, is the 
protein p21, which inhibits CDK1, 2, 4, and 6. Expression of p21 coincides with hyperacetylation of histones 
H3 and H4 in its promotor region. Overexpression of many HDACs (mainly the ones in class I) has shown to 
repress p21 in different cell types [22]. In prostate cancer cells, decrease in p21 expression has been caused 
by an over expression of HDAC1 and 3 [23, 
24]. Additionally, similar tendencies have 
been shown by increased expression of 
HDAC2 in gastric carcinomas, colorectal 
carcinomas, 
cervical 
dysplasias, 
and 
endometrial stromal sarcomas [25, 26, 27]. 
Inhibitor treatment and knockdown of these 
HDAC enzymes have shown to give increased 
levels of acetylations on histones H3 and H4, 
thus increasing the amount of p21 and 
ultimately leading to apoptosis of these 
cancerous cells and stopping their growth.   
There are many cancer cell promoting 
processes that are being triggered by 
CDK1 
CDK4, 6 
CDK2 
CDK2 
CDK2 
Cell cycle 
Figure 4  Cell cycle. G1 phase; cell grow in size, and prepare 
for copying its DNA. S phase; DNA is replicated. G2 
phase; the cell is preparing for division. M phase; the 
cell is dividing itself. CDKs enhance the movement 
of the cell from one phase to another. Inhibitor 
proteins such as p21, reduces the cells ability to pass 
the Restriction point. 
==PAGE:20==
6 
 
decreased acetylations at specific promoter regions on the cell genome, and the role of HDACI in these 
systems have been reviewed [28, 29, 30]. To conclude this section, a few more tendencies will be described.  
Loss of expression of proliferation-restraining genes is a common feature in cancer cells. Expression of 
members of the transcription factor GATA family, GATA4 and GATA6 is lost in ovarian cancer cells and has 
found to correlate with hypoacetylation of histone H3 and H4 at their respectively promoter regions. 
Treatment with HDAC inhibitor TSA, has shown to restore the GATA factor expression, including their tumor 
suppressor targets [31].  
I addition, to treat various types of cancers with HDACI, one of the most unique properties of these drug 
candidates, is their ability to selectively induce apoptosis in cancerous cells. In vitro studies have shown that 
transformed cells may be at least tenfold more sensitive to HDACI compared with normal cells, thus providing 
evidence that HDACI treatment can be used as tumor-cell-selective killers [32]. Expanding the knowledge of 
the mechanism of these enzymes in a cell system, and identification of subtype selective inhibitors can help 
to decrease the unavoidable side-effects even further. 
 
1.3 Fluorescence based HDAC activity 
Due to the increasing potential of HDAC inhibitors as new drugs for treatment of various types of diseases, 
the development of a simple and effective screening methodology has been of great interest. In 1999, 
Hoffmann et al. proposed a fluorescence-based HDAC activity assay [33]. The method exploited the 
fluorescent labelling of an α-N Boc protected lysine derivative with a 7-amino-4-methylcoumarin (AMC) to 
investigate the activity of HDAC enzymes, using HPLC with a fluorescent detector to determine the quantity 
of deacetylated substrate. The method proved to be applicable for both in vivo and in vitro studies, and was 
a great improvement of the former utilized methods, using H3 labeling of inhibitors and acetyl groups [34, 
35], since these methods lead to exposure and disposal of radioactive waste. Furthermore, difficulties with 
preparation and standardization hampered the effectiveness of these methods.  
A further development of the fluorescent-based method was conducted, and in 2000 Backes et al. developed 
a strategy using two Trypsin-like enzymes, plasmin and thrombin, to release the AMC group upon 
deacetylation, thus indirectly revealing the HDAC activity, without using HPLC for quantification [36].  
==PAGE:21==
7 
 
 
Figure 5  Schematic illustration of fluorogenic emission upon AMC cleavage, when using Trypsin-like protease enzymes.  
As shown in Figure 5, upon deacetylation of the ε-N AMC-lysine derivative, a recognition site for the Trypsin-
like deacetylase is created, thus cleaving the AMC group from the C-terminal site of the lysine. After excitation 
at 360 nm, a luminescence spectrometer can be used to determine the intensity of the emission at 460 nm. 
Assuming that the protease readily cleaves the deacetylated compound, the intensity of the emission of the 
AMC group is proportional to the activity of the HDAC enzyme. 
 
1.4 HDAC selectivity and inhibitors 
The biological processes of HDAC enzymes are not only restricted to the transcription of DNA, but are also 
directly or indirectly involved in many other systems, and the knowledge of HDAC biology is continuously 
progressed. Non-histone protein targets of HDACs include transcription factors, transcription regulators, 
signal transduction factors, chaperone proteins, structural proteins, inflammation mediators, and viral 
proteins. Lysine acetylation can either increase or decrease the function or stability of the proteins, or 
protein-protein interactions [37].  
 
Figure 6 Known lysine-modifying acyl groups, which may be reversed by HDACs or sirtuins [13]. 
==PAGE:22==
8 
 
It has been shown that many proteins may be acetylated on numerous lysine positions, thus showing that 
acetylation should be considered as a general post-translational modification, involved in protein regulation 
[38]. In addition, HDACs have been shown to deacetylate proteins such as p53 (regulates the cell cycle and 
functions as a tumor surpressor preventing cancer), E2F (involved in cell cycle regulation and synthesis of 
DNA in mammalian cells), α-tubulin (protein within the tubulin family, which make up microtubules), and 
MyoD (protein with a key role in regulating muscle differentiation), which illustrates the complex function of 
HDACs in many processes in the cell [39, 40].  Furthermore, recent studies have discovered a variety of 
different acyl groups, which could be lysine post-translational modifications as shown in Figure 6 [41]. 
Therefore, the activity of the individual HDAC isoforms towards these acylated lysine derivatives is of great 
interest, and resent in vitro studies have shown that HDAC3 show selectivity towards decrotonylation [42]. 
Furthermore, the studies showed that chain extension from the acetyl to propionyl and butyryl did not lead 
to a decrease in deacylation for HDAC3, contrary to other isoforms (HDAC1 and 6) [43]. 
A wide variety of natural and synthetic derivatives have been identified to be able to inhibit the activity of 
HDACs. The term HDACI (HDAC inhibitor) is commonly used for 
compounds that target the “classical” HDACs (class I, II, and IV). 
According to X-ray crystallographic findings and structure-activity 
relationships (SAR) of various inhibitor classes, it was suggested that 
the pharmacophore of HDACIs consists of a hydrophilic zinc-binding 
domain (also known as “the chelator”), which interacts with the 
active site Zn2+, a flat hydrophobic linker region, which occupies the 
channel, and a surface recognition site (typically hydrophobic, also known as “the cap”) [44] (see Figure 7). 
Substantial investigation on pair-wise comparison of the potency for the individual deacetylases, has 
revealed that thus far only one compound has been found that act as a non-selective HDAC inhibitor. The 
impact of linker substitution and geometry on target selectivity gives rise to the inhibitor selectivity between 
the different classes and individual enzymes [45].  
So far, two pharmaceutical HDAC inhibitors have been approved to be used in humans; SAHA (vorinostat, 
Merck Reachers Laboratories) [46] and FK-228 (romidepsin, Gloucester Pharmaceutical) [47], and more than 
twenty additional compounds are in advanced clinical trials [48].  Table 1 shows different HDAC inhibitors 
who are currently in different stages of clinical development, and their selectivity towards the different HDAC 
isoforms. As can be seen, none of the shown substrates show isoform selectivity, however FK-228 and MS-
275 show class-selectivity (class I). Furthermore, only very few of the compounds show inhibition of the class 
IIa, especially HDAC4 does not seem to be inhibited by any of the shown candidates. 
 
Figure 7  Pharmacophore of HDACI (SAHA). 
==PAGE:23==

==PAGE:24==

Clinical studies of non-isoform-selective (pan-selective) and class-selective HDACIs have revealed a 
comparable toxicity profile, including gastrointestinal disturbances, fatigue, and in several cases QT 
prolongation (prolonged or abnormal repolarisation), which are associated with an increased risk of fatal 
arrhythmia [53]. These clinical results and the knowledge about the structural differences between the 
different classes and isoforms, have stimulated the interests for creating isoform-selective compounds to 
minimize the toxic side effects, and targeting more specific diseases.  
The discovered substrates for HDAC inhibition have some structural similarities and functional groups 
including hydroxamates, cyclic peptides, benzamides, and aliphatic acids. The hydroxamic acid derivative TSA 
(see Figure 8) was the first natural product to inhibit the HDACs directly [54]. The corresponding carboxylate 
of TSA has shown to be ineffective as a HDACI, which indicates that the hydroxamate is required for inhibitor 
activity, hence the former mentioned chelate effect. Cyclic peptides do not consist of the traditional 
pharmacophore, but they are typically active on the nanomolar levels. The compound FK-228 is a natural 
prodrug, and is activated by cellular reduction of the disulphide bonds to the corresponding thiols.  Aliphatic 
acids such as valproic acid are known drugs that have been on the market for nononcological uses, but have 
shown to exhibit inhibition of HDACs. These drugs are weaker inhibitors than hydroxamic acids (or cyclic 
peptides), and several prodrugs have been developed to give the compounds more lipophilic character, thus 
giving a better membrane permeability [55].  
 
Another natural compound that has shown HDAC inhibition is Apicidin, which is a fungal metabolite. Apicidin 
consist of a cyclic tetrapeptide and a long alkyl chain with an ethyl ketone. The cyclic tetrapeptide interacts 
with the surface of the HDAC, and the ketone is potentially binding to Zinc [44]. The compound has shown to 
selectively inhibit HDAC1, 2, 3, and 8 (class I) [45].  
In the following, the role of HDACs and their respective isoforms and classes will be discussed with regard to 
observed tendencies and functional differences. 
 
Figure 8  Natural compounds Apicidin and TSA, that serves as HDAC inhibitors. 
==PAGE:25==
11 
 
1.4.1 Class I  
As mentioned in section 1.1, the HDAC class I includes HDAC1, 2, 3, and 8. They are primarily found in the 
nucleus, and are ubiquitously expressed. With the exception of HDAC8, the function of these enzymes are 
mediated as part of large macrocomplexes, and the structures often involve more than one HDAC subunit 
and are in association with corepressor proteins [56, 57]. HDAC1 and 2 interact with each other and form 
multiprotein complexes with intensive 3 (Sin3), nuclear receptor corepressor (N-CoR), nucleosome 
remodelling deacetylase (NuRD) corepressor of RE1-silencing transcription (CoREST) or silencing mediator of 
retinoic acid and thyroid hormone receptors (SMRT). It has been suggested that the formation of these homo 
and/or heterodimers are necessary for their catalytic activity. Furthermore, formation of these complexes 
enables their ability to interact with DNA sequence-specific transcription factors to repress transcription. 
Crystal structures of all of the HDAC class I enzymes have been solved [58, 59, 60, 61]. The complexes showed 
great similarity, however HDAC3 showed to have a solvent-exposed tyrosine located adjacent to the active 
site, which may give rise to substrate selectivity. HDAC8 is a member of class I due to the sequence similarity, 
but the enzyme differs in several aspects and can be considered as a borderline between class I and II [62]. 
In contrary to the other class I HDAC isoforms, HDAC8 is expressed in specific tissues, and shows deacetylase 
activity as a single polypeptide, due to the absence of the 50–111 amino acid C-terminal domain, which is 
required for HDAC1–3 to recruit and form protein complexes [63]. HDAC8 also differ from the other isoforms 
in the shape and dimensions of the cavity in the foot pocket, by the replacement of a leucine to a tryptophan, 
which prevent binding of more bulky moieties in that area [17]. Furthermore, HDAC8 has a highly flexible 
active site and a quite unique subpocket. This enable the design of HDAC8 selective inhibitors with more 
bulky substituents in the cap/beginning of the linker region, which cannot be docked into the active site of 
the other class I HDAC isoforms due to steric clash [64]. One of such is the compound 3 (see Figure 9), which 
contain a quite large hydrophobic substituent. This compound has shown IC50 values of 0.027, 3, and 3 μM 
against HDAC8, 1 and 3 respectively, and no activity towards HDAC2, 4, 6, 10, and 11 [65]. 
 
Figure 9 Examples of subtype selective inhibitors of class I HDAC enzymes. 1 has shown HDAC1 and 2 
selectivity. 2 has shown HDAC3 selectivity. 3 has shown HDAC8 selectivity. The (2-amino
phenyl)alkyl-amide group is highlighted in red, and is the Zink-binding domain of these inhibitors.
==PAGE:26==
12 
 
Despite the great similarity among HDAC1–3, HDACIs with a preference for one or two of these isoforms have 
been described in the literature. No HDAC1 or 2 selective inhibitors have been found yet, but compounds 
such as 1 have shown IC50 values of 0.05, 0.2 and >20 μM against HDAC1, 2 and 3 respectively [66]. Other 
compounds, which also are based on the (2-amino phenyl)alkyl-amide group have shown great HDAC3 
selectivity. Compounds 2 exhibited an IC50 value of 0.08 μM for HDAC3, and showed no inhibition of any 
other HDAC up to 15 μM [67].  
 
1.4.2 Class IIa 
Contrary to class I and class IIb, the class IIa (HDAC4, 5, 7, and 9) are quite inefficient on standard acetylated 
substrates, which is typically associated with the role of HDAC enzymes [68]. The main reason for this 
phenomenon might be due to the replacement of the tyrosine, which is considered to play an important role 
as a transition-state stabilizer (see mechanism Figure 3), by histidine. This substitution causes decreased 
catalytic activity towards deacetylation, which indicates that these enzymes may target compounds with 
bulkier moieties adjacent to the Zinc-binding domain, including other acyl-groups as mentioned in section 
1.3. High-throughput screening has also revealed that substrates functionalized with the more sterically 
demanding ε-N-trifluoroacetyl lysine (Ktfa) group are readily hydrolyzed by the class IIa enzymes [45]. The 
Ktfa is of cause more easily hydrolyzed compared to the traditional Kac, due to the effect of the inductively 
electron withdrawing trifluoromethyl group, but the class IIa enzymes showed markedly faster kinetics, and 
HDAC1 and HDAC6 showed no deacylase activity towards this type of compound, presumably due to steric 
interactions.  
 
 
Figure 10 Compound 4 and 5 are class IIa selective HDACIs [69, 70]. A and B show the representation of compound 5 in HDAC7,
and how it interacts with the active site Zinc domain. 
==PAGE:27==
13 
 
Examples of selective class IIa inhibitors have been described. The first one (4) was introduced in 2009 by 
Tessier et al. [69], which revealed a rather uncommon pharmacophore as shown in Figure 10, but other 
compounds such as 5 have also shown class IIa selectivity [70]. The crystal structure of compound 5 co-
complexed in HDAC7 has revealed that the triflouromethyloxadiazole moiety bind to the Zinc atom in a new 
binding mode. The Zinc ion interacts with both the fluorine from the electron withdrawing trifluoromethyl 
group, and the oxygen on the oxadiazole heterocycle (see Figure 10, A and B). The discovery of this new type 
of binding  has been described as a new paradigm in the design of metalloenzyme inhibitors, since it enable 
one to selectively engineer other parts of a molecule if only much weaker interactions with the target are 
available.  
 
1.4.3 Class IIb 
Class IIb consists of HDAC6 and 10. These two enzymes are quite unique since they contain two deacetylase 
domains. However, in HDAC10 only one of the two catalytic domains is functionally working, whereas both 
domains work independently in HDAC6. As shown in Figure 11, homology models have shown that there are 
substantial differences in the catalytic channel between HDAC1 and HDAC6 [71]. This extra space enable 
compounds that contain quite rigid and large cap moieties to reach the catalytic area, which would not fit 
into the HDAC1 enzyme. Compounds without large cap moieties have also been shown to exhibit HDAC6 
selectivity such as compound 6 (Figure 12). It was suggested that the meta-phenethyl carboxamide resides 
in a deep secondary hydrophobic pocket, which is formed via a conformational change in Phe139, thus 
leading to increased potency of this specific substrate [72].  
Tubacin (Figure 12) has also shown to selectively inhibit HDAC6 activity. It does not affect the acetylation of 
histones or inhibit cell cycle progression, however it causes an accumulation of acetylated α-tubulin [73]. 
Figure 11    Homology model showing the catalytic tunnel of HDAC1 and HDAC6 [71]. 
==PAGE:28==
14 
 
Computational studies of binding interactions of tubacin in HDAC1, 6, and 8, compared to similar 
investigations with SAHA, have shown that the interactions of Tubacin are mainly lipophilic and/or non-
bonded, and hence being most efficient in a narrow pocket, as in the case of HDAC6 [74]. 
 
HDAC10 is the least characterized member of the class II HDAC enzymes and no selective HDACIs for HDAC10 
has been found yet. HDAC10 has shown to interact with HDAC3, like other members of the class II HDACs 
(HDAC4, 5, and 7), which indicates that beside the sequence similarity of these enzymes, HDAC10 also share 
their functional properties [75]. A molecular model study has revealed that the behaviour of the tunnels 
towards the catalytic site in the different HDAC isoforms vary significantly, and it was shown that the tunnel 
in HDAC10 was very wide compared to other members of class I and IV (HDAC8 and HDAC11) [76].  
 
1.4.4 Class IV 
HDAC11 is structurally related to both class I and II, and has therefore been placed in its own class (class IV). 
It is the least investigated isoform of all of the classical HDACs, although it has been reported that selective 
depletion of this isoform, which is overexpressed in several carcinomas, causes cell death and inhibit 
metabolic activity in various cancer cell lines [77]. Furthermore, depletion in normal cells did not induce any 
changes in metabolic activities and viabilities.  
 
1.5 Acetylated profiling studies of HDAC enzymes 
As of now, only limited information has been reported on the pharmacology of HDAC10 and 11, which might 
be due to the lack of efficient substrates for these two isoforms [42]. Catalytic efficiencies and inhibitor 
selectivity profiling data obtained from in vitro experiments with recombinant enzymes may not always 
reflect the behaviour of those enzymes in their native environment, which often involve multiprotein 
Figure 12   HDAC6 selective inhibitors. 
==PAGE:29==
15 
 
complexes in vivo. However, in vitro screening of these enzymes remains important for discovery and 
evaluation of new inhibitors as well as for mechanistic investigation.  
Extensive studies of varying sequences of small ε-N acetylated peptides have been conducted to identify the 
recognition site of the enzymes and gather information about how it reflects the selectivity of the individual 
HDAC isoforms [78, 79]. Variations on both the C- and N-terminal side of the acetylated lysine (H-AA1-AA2-
Lys(Ac)-AA3-AA4-OH) has been conducted. For the N-terminal investigations, the AMC based fluorogenic 
method was utilized, but this method was not applicable for the C-terminal investigation, thus exploring 
cysteine derivered peptide fragments immobilization to a self-assembled monolayer of alkanethiolates on 
gold. The results of these findings revealed that HDAC1, 3, and 6 disfavoured amino acids with charged 
sidechains at the AA2-position. However, other studies have pointed to the fact that all of the HDAC isoforms 
show favourable interactions when having an arginine at the AA2-position [80]. HDAC1, 3, and 6 seemed to 
favour Trp, Phe, Lue, Ile, Val, Gly, Tyr, and His at the AA2-position, and Ile, and Leu at the AA1-position. Other 
particular amino acid combinations showed activity contrary to the expectations, such as; Asp-Gln-Lys(Ac) 
for HDAC3, or Lys-Glu-Lys(Ac) and Glu-Glu-Lys(Ac) for HDAC6. For the carboxylic positions AA3 and AA4, it 
was found that HDAC2, 3 and 8 preferred amino acids with aromatic sidechains, such as Phe, Tyr or Trp, but 
Arg also showed some activity at the AA3 position. At the AA4-position, residues with Lys, Arg, His, Trp, Phe, 
Leu, or Gly showed to be most active.  
In summary, it was found that peptide sequences containing amino acids with aromatic, hydrophobic or basic  
sidechains adjacent to the acetylated lysine are most active towards deacetylation. In comparison to these 
findings, it has been found that the most frequently occurring residues adjacent to acetylated lysines on non-
histone substrates are Tyr and Phe [81].  
 
==PAGE:30==
16 
 
1.6 Solid phase peptide synthesis 
Solid phase peptide synthesis (SPPS) was first introduced in 1963 and was pioneered by Nobel Prize winning 
Robert Bruce Merrifield [82]. The ability synthesize peptides on a solid support revolutionized the 
effectiveness of creating a variety of peptides in varying lengths, compared to the solution-based methods. 
The coupling procedures are essentially the same in solution- and solid-phase, but the work-up procedure 
after each coupling and deprotection is far more efficient and beneficial when creating new peptides on a 
solid support.  The general principle of SPPS is shown in Figure 13. The resin is an insoluble polymer to which 
small building blocks (typically nucleotides or amino acids) can be attached. 
To be able to selectively cleave a synthesized compound, these substrates are connected via a linker piece. 
α-N-protected C-terminal amino-acid residues are anchored via their carbonyl group to a hydroxyl or an 
amino resin to yield an ester or an amide, that will produce a C-terminal acid or C-terminal amide peptide 
respectively, when being cleaved. Once the first amino acid has been coupled to the linker, the desired 
peptide sequence is developed in a linear fashion from C- to N-terminus. In the design of a synthetic route 
towards a desired peptide, there are two types of protecting groups; the permanent and the temporary (see 
Figure 13). The permanent protecting groups (PPG) are primarily positioned to mask the reactivity of the 
Figure 13 Schematic illustration of the basic principles of SPPS. Y = O or NH. TPG = temporary protecting group. PPG = permanent 
protecting group. 
==PAGE:31==
17 
 
functional groups on the side-chains and are stable in the reaction conditions used during the peptide 
elongation. The temporary protecting group (TPG) at α-N can be removed selectively under mild conditions. 
There are two different strategies that are being used in SPPS; the Fmoc/tBu and the Boc/Bzl. The latter relies 
on the difference in acid lability between the temporary protecting group Boc, which is typically cleaved in a 
mixture of TFA and CH2Cl2, while the permanent sidechain protecting groups such as Bzl, Cy, and Ts, require 
treatment with stronger acids such as hydrogen fluoride. Complications with the use of the toxic hydrogen 
fluoride and the need for special polytetrafluoroethylene-lined apparatus, have limited the ease and utility 
of this method, and have additionally shown to partially degrade PEG based resins. The other milder 
Fmoc/tBu method uses the base labile temporary α-N protecting group Fmoc, which is typically cleaved in 
20% piperidine in DMF, and acid labile permanent protecting groups such as tBu, Boc, and Trt. The milder 
deprotections and the cleavage conditions of the desired peptide, has led the Fmoc-based method to be the 
method of choice for the routine synthesis when performing SPPS. 
The reactions of solid phase synthesis (SPS) are most often occurring in a flat-bottomed syringe, equipped 
with a filter, which allow all excess reagents to be removed by filtration and washings. In addition, large 
excess of reagents in high concentrations are used to drive the coupling reactions to completion. The 
efficiency of these purifications, work-ups, and general coupling procedures, is what gives SPPS its unique 
performance, and has permitted the development of automatized robotic instrumentation, that can create 
20-mer peptide sequences overnight.  
 
1.6.1 Solid supports 
When Merrifield first introduced the synthesis on solid support, the resin beads were based on a  
chloromethylated polymer of styrene with 2 percent divinylbenzene (DVB) cross-linkage [82] (see Figure 14). 
These beads allowed the diffusion of reagents when the polymer was swelled in appropriate solvents and 
enabled Merrifield to anchor functional groups to the solid support. The swelling process is an attempt to 
dissolve the polymer, and since most of the reactions takes place inside the beads, the resin must swell to a 
degree that allows the reactions to be performed, without disrupting the mechanical stability of the resin. 
The amount of cross-linking alters the swelling properties of a polymeric matrix and nowadays these 
polystyrene (PS) backbones are typically cross-linked with 1–2% DVB.  
==PAGE:32==
18 
 
 
Even though it has been more than 50 years since Merrifield presented the PS-DVB resin, many solid supports 
with very similar structures are still widely used. However, it has been shown that when increasing the 
peptide length, the incorporation of specific amino acids decreases. In an attempt to solve this problem, 
different systems were incorporated into the PS polymer with more polar functionalities, such as the 
polyethylene glycol (PEG) moiety. The PEG was introduced to increase the swelling and Czarnik et al. showed 
that by increasing the amount PEG chains cross-linked to a PS resin, faster kinetics were observed [83]. This 
realization led to the development of a variety of PEG-containing resins. One of the most well-established 
PEG-functionalized PS resins is the TentaGel, which is prepared by grafting 50–70% of PEG to a low cross-
linked PS polymer by an ether linkage. These types of solid supports showed to be highly suited for synthesis 
of longer peptides, and showed great swelling properties in a variety of relevant polar solvents. However, 
the benzylic ether PS-linkage showed to be unstable to strong acidic reagents, which led to the development 
of the ArgoGel [84]. The graft component of ArgoGel is a bifunctional PEG chain attached to a PS core with 
an aliphatic linkage, which made the resin inert to the presence of strong acids and nucleophilic reagents. To 
further enhance the polarity of the solid support, pure cross-linked PEG resins with no content of polystyrene 
has been developed by Meldal [85], and included poly(ethylene glycol)-poly-(N,N-dimethylacrylamide) 
copolymer (PEGA). A unique advantage of PEGA resins is that they swell very well in water, and peptide 
substrates anchored to PEGA can be used in interaction studies with proteins and enzymes [86]. Another 
more recent developed resin is the ChemMatrix [87]. The ChemMatix resin primarily contains ether bonds 
and is more highly cross-linked, thus giving a more stable resin with increased loadings.  
Figure 14    Illustration of some of the most commonly used resins in SPPS, including both PS and 
PEG based. X = OH or NH2.  
==PAGE:33==
19 
 
In general, the PEG-based resins have shown to outperform the PS-based, both regarding reaction kinetics, 
when synthesizing medium-length to long-length peptides, and when synthesizing peptides with “difficult 
sequences”. However, considering this, the PEG-based resins have also shown to be more costly. 
 
1.6.2 Linkers 
The moiety that ensure covalent bonding between a small organic molecule (which in most cases are 
protected amino acid derivatives) and a solid support is termed a linker group, as it will serve as a molecular 
bridge between these two components. Since the linkage between the organic substrate and the resin needs 
to remain intact for the duration of the synthesis, the linker needs to be stable under a wide range of reaction 
conditions required for a continuous synthesis of a target molecule or peptide. However, cleavage conditions 
should be rapid, efficient, and not damaging to the final product. Due to the requirements for a successful 
linker, great effort and attention has been given to create optimal linker moieties for specific designs and 
synthetic strategies. Hundreds of linkers have been developed, which in total exhibit a wide variety of 
cleavage conditions, complexity, and stability [88], but only a few of these will be discussed in the following 
section (see Figure 15).  
 
Linkers are divided into two categories; integral- and nonintegral-linkers [89]. Integral-linkers are classified 
as linkers which form a part with the solid support core (e.g. the Merrifield linker). The integral-linkers were 
very popular in the early days of solid phase synthesis, but the disadvantage of carrying out the synthetic 
steps directly on the resin was affected by steric clash and electronic effects, which influenced the overall 
outcome. Nonintegral-linkers (also known as grafted-linkers) are on the other hand linkers, which are being 
Figure 15 Presentation of different linkers in SPPS including the acid labile Rink, Trityl, and Wang, 
nucleophilically cleaved HMBA, and photolabile Holmes. R-substituent of the trityl linker 
include; Cl ant the 2-position, and Cl, OMe, and Me at the 4-position. X = OH or NH2. 
==PAGE:34==
20 
 
attached to the resin core, typically via ethers, amides, or C-C bonds. The Wang linker is one of the most 
commonly used linkers and it is prepared by reacting 4-hydroxybenzyl alcohol with the Merrifield resin in the 
presence of methoxide [90]. In addition to these nonintegral-linkers is the unloaded linkers, which are 
prepared in solution, typically by standard coupling procedures, but they require resins containing free 
amines or alcohols.  
The most commonly used linkers in SPPS are acid labile and the degree of their lability depend on the nature 
of the linker component. The Wang resin makes use of a para-oxygen atom within the benzylic alcohol linker, 
to stabilize the formed carbocation upon cleavage under acidic conditions such as TFA/CH2Cl2 (1:1, v/v). A 
similar cleavage mechanism and conditions are proceeded in the use of the Rink amide linker [91]. This 
unloaded linker is introduced as a Fmoc-protected derivative through an amide coupling, and can be used to 
synthesize amides. Even milder acidic cleavage conditions can be applied by using the trityl linkers. These 
linkers can be used to cleave functional groups such as alcohols, hydroxamic acids, amines, thiols, and acids, 
and are often suitable for producing side-chain-protected peptides, but they require scavengers such as 
triisopropylsilane (TIS) when being cleaved [89].  
Nucleophilically cleaved linkers include the 4-hydroxymethylbenzoic acid (HMBA) linker. The ester linkage of 
this linker is easily cleaved by conditions such as 0.1 M NaOH and display complete stability to the acidic 
conditions that are used in SPPS for the Fmoc/tBu strategy.  
Photolabile linkers such as Holmes photolabile linker can be cleaved under very mild conditions through 
irradiation at 360 nm, and are typically stabile towards both acidic and basic conditions. The yield from these 
cleavages are often not quantitative and it has been shown that solid supported photolysis are considerably 
slower than the solution based, which is due to light scattering, shielding, and shadowing effects [92]. These 
linkers have however shown great utility in bead based biological assays, and subsequent analysis by MALDI-
TOF for compound identification in combinatorial peptide libraries [93]. These photolabile linkers (and 
especially those developed by Holmes and Jones [94]), will be discussed in further detail in section 2.1.1.  
 
1.6.3 Formation of amide bond by usage of coupling reagents 
Amide bonds are typically synthesized from the union of carboxylic acids and amines through a condensation, 
but the unification of these two functional groups does not occur spontaneously at ambient temperature. 
For this reason, the coupling of two amino acids to form a peptide bond usually require activation of the 
carboxylic acid, by converting the –OH group from the acid into a good leaving group prior to the addition of 
the amine. This process can be accomplished by using coupling reagents to form acid chlorides, anhydrides, 
==PAGE:35==
21 
 
carbonic anhydrides or activated esters [95]. Unfortunately, the activation of the carboxylic acid also enables 
several transition states, which can potentially lead to loss of chirality at the carboxylic residue undergoing 
the activation step [96]. As shown in Figure 16, two base-catalysed pathways can lead to racemization of the 
peptide residue. Racemization during coupling reactions are mainly caused by the formation of the 5(4H)-
oxazolone (Path A). The formation of this intermediate correlates with the activation potential of the leaving 
group X and the electronic properties of the N-acyl residue. The mesomeric electron donation by the amino 
group enhances the nucleophilic attack of the oxygen atom, and acyl residues such as acetyl, benzoyl, or 
trifluoroacetyl groups, favour this oxazolone formation. The racemization occurs at basic conditions via an 
aromatic transition state, which gives rise to a mixture of both enantiomers. Carbamate protecting groups 
such as Fmoc, Boc, Cbz, or Alloc, decrease the likelihood of oxazolone formation.  
 
Direct enolization (Path B) is the other cause of racemization in peptide synthesis. This process is also base 
catalysed and rely on the acidity of the α-proton, which is affected by the electron-withdrawing effects of 
the R-sidechain, the acyl group on the amine, and the activation group X. Factors affecting both racemization 
paths are the polarity of the solvent, temperature, and the chemical properties of the base [97].  
The first coupling reagents to be synthesized were carbodiimides, and reagents such as the 
dicyclohexylcarbodiimide (DCC, see Figure 17) was first reported as a coupling reagent in 1955 [98]. It was 
realized that oxazolone intermediates were formed after activation and generation of the O-acylurea 
Figure 16 Two base catalysed paths of racemization observed in SPPS, including 
formation of an aromatic trasitionstate after formation of the 5(4H)-oxazolone
(Path A), and direct enolization (Path B). 
==PAGE:36==
22 
 
intermediate when using these coupling conditions, thus leading to racemization and loss of chirality. To 
reduce this undesired tendency and to increase the yield, the additives 1-hydroxy-1H-benzotriazole (HOBt), 
and 1-hydroxy-7-azabenzotrizole (HOAt) were introduced [99].  The effect of these additives, is that they 
readily react with the activated acid to give an OBt or an OAt active ester, which are less reactive than the 
corresponding O-acylurea, but reactive enough to undergo the desired amide formation at ambient 
temperature. 
  
HOAt is generally a better activation component than HOBt, due to the nitrogen atom located at the 7-
position of the azabenzotriazole, which is provided by a double effect. First, the electron-withdrawing effect 
of the nitrogen atom improves its ability to work as a leaving group, but more significantly, the nitrogen 
stabilises the formed transition state through hydrogen bonding to the reacting amine upon the nucleophilic 
attack (this is illustrated in the HATU mechanism, see Figure 18). HOAt and OAt-containing coupling reagents 
are typically more expensive than their OBt counterparts, and safety considerations must also be taken into 
account when performing synthesis with these components, since they are considered to have explosive 
properties, due to the high content of nitrogen.  
 
Figure 17   Commonly used coupling reagents and additives used in SPPS.  
Figure 18 Mechanism of coupling using HATU coupling reagent, including the stabilised transition state upon the nucleophilic attack 
of an amine. 
==PAGE:37==
23 
 
Several coupling reagents based on these 1-H-benzotriazoles have been developed including uranium/ 
aminum, phosphonium, and immonium salts, and a few of these will be discussed in the following. All of 
these so called “in situ” coupling reagents, rely on the same basic mechanism, which include activation of the 
carboxylic acid followed by a readily formation of an activated ester, and finally a nucleophilic attack from 
the amine to form the desired amide bond (Figure 18). These coupling reagents were produced to create 
ideal coupling conditions for a wide variety of substrates, and reduce both the reaction time and 
racemization. One of the first phosphonium reagents reported was the BOP reagent [100], but its use has 
been limited due to the formation of the carcinogenic and toxic hexamethylphosphoric triamide byproduct. 
This led to the development of the commonly used pyrrolidino derivative, PyBOP, which proved to be more 
reactive than BOP [101]. 
For the uranium/aminum reagents, the two coupling reagents HBTU and HATU are typically the reagents of 
choice and are based on the HOBt and HOAt respectively. In this project the similar coupling reagent, TBTU, 
has been used, which only differ from HBTU by its counter-anion, but studies have shown that this had no 
practical influence on the outcome of these coupling reactions [95].  
For sterically more hindered amines such as secondary amines, the use of acid halides have been widely used. 
For more than hundred years ago, the first synthesis of a dipeptide using acid chlorides was reported. The 
acid chloride was generated from reagents such as thionyl chloride and phosphorus pentachloride. These 
coupling conditions are quite harsh, and are not compatible with many protecting groups. Therefore, great 
effort has gone into creating milder conditions for in situ formed acid halides, which are compatible with the 
generally applied building blocks in SPPS. Worth mentioning is the reagent PyBroP [102] that forms acid 
bromides, and has been described as being the reagent of choice when performing difficult couplings with 
hindered and relatively unreactive amines. In this project, the more reactive species triphosgene (also 
referred to as BTC) has been used when coupling with secondary amines [103]. Triphosgene generates the 
highly toxic phosgene in situ and as the name suggests, three equivalents of phosgene is formed per 
triphosgene unit. A drawback when using these coupling reagents is the formation of these highly activated 
acids, which leads to increased formation of the oxazolone intermediate and racemization. 
==PAGE:38==
24 
 
1.7 Bead based screening technologies  
 
The basic idea of screening ligands directly on beads was recognized by Lam et al. as early as 1991 [104]. 
Many steps have since been taken to realize this concept and reported progress range from optimization of 
screening conditions to the development of suitable library decoding techniques. The efficiency of these 
bead-based screening assays rely on the fact, that they can be used to screen thousands of compounds in 
one single setup by utilizing the “split-and-mix” synthesis (The principle is presented in Figure 19). The split-
and-mix approach was first described in 1991 and was 
proposed as a rapid method to synthesize several 
peptides, followed by HPLC purification [105]. Adapting 
these findings to the bead screening technology, only 
one peptide was displayed within one single bead, 
hence the full coupling of one peptide during each 
coupling step. Using an appropriate detection method 
and assay setup, one can visualize and isolate a bead 
containing a specific peptide that gives rise to a desired 
response through an interaction with an enzyme or an 
antibody. This method is known as the one-bead-one-
compound (OBOC) method, and has been used on 
different targets, such as antibodies, proteases, 
kinases, G-protein coupled receptors, nuclear extracts, 
bacteria, and whole cells [106]. In the beginning, the 
libraries were synthesized on PS-PEG based TentaGel 
resins, due to their ability to swell in water, but later 
stages in the development process revealed that more 
porous PEGA beads were more suited for the library 
construction, because they allowed enzymes to gain 
access to the bead interior [107].  
 
Figure 19 Basic principle of “split-and-mix” synthesis 
using amino acid building blocks; Glu, Phe, 
and Lys to synthesize 27 different tri- 
peptides. Adapted from Furka et al. [105]. 
==PAGE:39==
25 
 
1.7.1 Screening methods 
For high throughput screening of one-bead-one-compound combinatorial libraries, both solid- and solution-
based assays have been developed. In the solid-based assay, the ligands are still covalently attached to the 
solid support, and the active assay component binds to the resin bound ligand. For identification of the active 
beads within a large group of beads, the beads containing an active component, must be recognized by 
visualization. The basic principle of HTS using OBOC libraries is shown in Figure 20. 
Meldal et al. introduced the fluorogenic quench, where the substrates on the active beads contained a 
quencher and upon cleavage, the quencher was removed, thus resulting in the appearance of a fluorescent 
signal [107]. Other methods have relied on using dyed or radioactive labelled targets, or antibody specific 
targets (either by epitope recognition or biotinylated) [108]. A drawback of on-bead assays is the inherent 
attachment of the compound to the solid support, which may not display the same activity as that of the free 
molecule in solution. For example, immobilized molecules may exhibit unfavourable kinetics on binding to 
the target protein due to the interference by the linker or solid support, which may lead to false negatives 
(an illustration of the different bead based screening technologies is presented in Figure 21).  
For the solution-based method, only a few screening assays have been described thus far. In one of the first 
solution based methods described, the compound-beads were immobilized in a thin layer of soft agar 
together with cancer cells, and after cleavage from the linker, the compounds were released and diffused 
slowly outward [109]. Inhibition was then detected by addition of a 3-(4,5-dimethylthazol-2-yl)-2,5-
diphenyltetrazolium bromide, which only stained the living cells. Other groups have adapted the use of the 
Figure 20     Illustration of the basic principle behind OBOC solid based screening.  
==PAGE:40==
26 
 
solution based screening methods in 
cell-based formats, by using a more 
biocompatible 
photo 
release 
followed by diffusion of the active 
compounds 
to 
identify 
novel 
antimicrobial, 
and 
antibacterial 
agents [110]. These in situ releasable 
solution phase assays are believed to 
have great potential, but will require 
further development before they can be reliably used for drug discovery.  A major issue is that the 
compounds do not diffuse freely out of all beads into the surrounding media and the use of gels is limited by 
the fact that the assay environment is determined by the nature of the gel. To address this solution-based 
diffusion problem, the OBOC libraries have been developed to being screened in microtiter plates, thus 
screening each bead in individual wells [111]. However, the beads that normally are being used for OBOC 
assays, contains approximately 100 pmol of compound, which is often not a sufficient quantity for a single 
bead biological assay. To compensate for this, larger macrobeads (500 μm) have been used. These larger 
beads have shown to yield at least 100 nmol of compound per bead, which of cause makes them more 
suitable for this type of microtiter HTS, but the increased bead size has also been associated with incomplete 
couplings, because the reagent diffusion will become a limiting factor.  
 
1.7.2 Analysis of the active beads 
Upon visual identification of a “hit” bead, the next step in the process is to identify the peptide sequence 
positioned on that specific bead. Given the fact that only approximately 100 pmol of compound is present in 
a single bead, extremely sensitive analytical methods are required for these hit identifications. One of the 
most commonly used methods is the very sensitive mass spectrometry, which require detachment of the 
bead substrate prior to the analysis. Depending on the linker attachment, the active compounds can be 
cleaved by acidic or basic conditions, or being photolytically released to avoid any solution based cleavage in 
a separate vessel. Using MS/MS analysis, the peptide fragments, which are typically cleaved at the peptide 
bond, can be analysed to identify the peptide sequence. One of the drawbacks of using mass spectrometry 
as an analytical tool, is that the signal intensity depends on the ionizability of each component of the mixture, 
which can be very different.  
 
Figure 21 Schematic illustration of the different screening technologies, 
including the solid based on-bead, and the two solution based 
methods; off-bead and in-bead. 
==PAGE:41==
27 
 
1.7.3 In-bead HTS technology  
An alternative to the two former mentioned bead screening technologies is the recently patented in-bead 
HTS technology [112]. The method relies on 
screening inside polymer beads, by utilizing the 
physical 
effect, 
that 
organic 
compounds 
preferably 
stay 
localized 
in 
the 
organic 
environment of the resin bead, when lying in an 
aqueous buffer, as shown in Figure 22.  By 
photolytically releasing the active compound in an aqueous buffer, each bead serve as a screening chamber, 
giving ideal conditions for off-bead solution based screening. In addition, the screening method is not 
hampered by the need of dividing each single bead out into individual wells prior to their screen, thus 
enabling off-bead screening of thousands of beads at the time.  
The aim of this project is to use the in-bead HTS technology to identify selective substrates for the histone 
deacetylase enzymes. The project is based on preliminary studies, demonstrating the usage of the in-bead 
HTS technology to identify novel inhibitors for these HDAC enzymes. The assay was carried out by adapting 
the methodology of a classic HDAC inhibitor setup using HeLa nuclear extract, where HDAC enzymes act on 
tailored substrates to liberate a fluorescent dye. These studies showed that upon photolytically cleavage of 
beads functionalized with the HDAC inhibitor SAHA, pronounced inhibition was observed (Figure 23 (A1/A2)). 
Furthermore, it was shown that no active beads were detected when the photolysis step was excluded 
(B1/B2), and beads functionalized ligands without any HDAC inhibitor compound, resulted in no quenching 
of the fluorescence (C1/C2). Another significant advantage of this screening method is that the substrate 
concentration in the beads can be controlled by the length of the irradiation period during the assay 
preparation, which allow discovery of high-affinity ligands.  
Figure 22 Effect of photo released compounds from resin beads 
in aqueous buffer and organic solvent. 
Figure 23 Visualization of functionalized beads at their brightfield and corresponding fluorescence microscopic images 
after 10 X magnification. A1/A2; beads functionalised with HDAC inhibitor SAHA. B1/B2; beads where the
photolysis step is excluded. C1/C2; beads functionalised with compound without any inhibitor effect. 
==PAGE:42==
