==PAGE:43==
29 
 
2 Results and discussion 
 
As the main title indicates, the aim for this project is to use the in-bead HTS technology to give a profiling of 
the individual HDAC subtypes, by identifying novel and highly active substrates. To date the study of HDAC 
substrates has been performed for two reasons. First, to discover how the individual isoform respond to the 
different amino acids adjacent to the acetylated lysine, and secondly, how they respond to the different types 
of acylations. The latter has been conducted to investigate if the individual isoform showed some type of 
selective cleavage of any post-translational modification, or if high activity could be gained for otherwise 
non-reactive isoforms. For example, the ε-N trifluoroacetylated (Ktfa) lysine derivatives have shown great 
deacylation for the class IIa enzymes, which has proven to be quite unreactive with the standard acetylated 
substrates in vitro. Ktfa is not a natural occurring PTM, but these substrates have been suggested as being 
potential substrates for the assay setup, when identifying class IIa inhibitors. The substrates that are currently 
being used in the fluorogenic inhibitor assay kits, are based on the residue 379-382 of tumor suppressor 
protein p53 as either Ac-Arg-His-Lys-Lys(Ac) or Ac-Arg-His-Lys(Ac)-Lys(Ac). These substrates have shown to 
be generally applicable for the class I enzymes (HDAC1, 2, 3, and 8) and the class IIb enzyme HDAC6, but are 
also used for HDAC10. Despite the great amounts of both recombinant enzyme and substrate, the 
fluorescence intensity of activity studies with HDAC10 are quite low relative to similar activity setups from 
other isoforms. Therefore, more sufficient deacetylase substrates are desirable for the general inhibitor assay 
setup (this is also the case for the class VI enzyme HDAC11). In addition, identification of selective substrates 
can assist the development of new and selective HDAC inhibitors, by displaying what type of steric 
interactions and electronic effects that are tolerated in the individual subtype.  
Figure 24 Illustration of the development process of a new setup for the in-bead HTS technology divided into four 
main steps. 
==PAGE:44==
30 
 
To be able to identify such substrates through a bead based high throughput screening method, a unique 
design needed to be developed. The overall development of this concept has been divided into a series of 
steps, including a solution based development of a photo-cleavable linker, a solid based split-and-mix library 
development of approximately 60,000 compounds, identification of “hit” beads in an in-bead assay by 
MS/MS analysis, and resynthesis of hit-bead followed by a solution based screening of the peptide structure 
(see Figure 24). The overall outcome of this methodology is depending on careful optimization of each of 
these steps.  
 
2.1 Linker development 
When reviewing the synthetic strategy for the linker development, certain criteria needed to be fulfilled to 
ensure an efficient library development and screening.  
 
The active substrates must be cleaved photolytically, thus enabling release in a biocompatible 
environment.  
 
No post solution based synthetic modifications must be required after cleavage from the bead.  
 
The acylated ε-amine should not be exposed while being covalently bonded to the resin, hence all 
enzyme-substrate interactions should be carried out in solution. This ensures more reliable post-
solution based hit results, and enables one to control the substrate concentration by adjusting the 
irradiation period. Furthermore, if the target enzyme cannot reach the substrate while being bound 
to the solid support, no undesired on-bead hits will be observed. 
 
Synthetic modifications on the two handles (the lysine α-N and ε-N) should be carried out selectively 
as the last steps of the overall synthetic route, for creating optimal conditions for a fast and efficient 
library development.  
Scheme 1 Retrosynthetic analysis for the development of a photolabile linker construct that can release the modified lysine 
derivatives upon irradiation. 
==PAGE:45==
31 
 
To meet these criteria, a retrosynthetic analysis was developed, as shown in Scheme 1. The core of the target 
substrates contains an ε-N acylated lysine derivative, with a 7-methyl-4-aminocoumarin (AMC) to the C-
terminal, and varying peptide chains on the α-N. The substrates are anchored to the Holmes photolinker via 
the lysine ε-N, and by having different protecting groups on the two amines, it enables one to selectively 
functionalize these two handles.   
 
2.1.1 Photolinker synthesis  
The 4,5-dialkoxy-2-nitrobenzyl group has been used for photolytic cleavage of numerous functionalized 
compounds from solid support as a linker construct, such as carboxamides, sulphonamides, carboxylic acids, 
alcohols, amines, and NH-1,2,3-triazoles [113]. This photolabile linker for SPS was pioneered by Yoo and 
Greenberg, and Holmes and Jones, exploiting the aldehyde and ketone respectively [114, 94]. The influence 
of the substituents on the benzylic center, is a complex combination of both steric interactions and electronic 
effects. As can be seen in Figure 25, a benzylic radical is formed after irradiation (7c). Introducing a methyl 
group on this position, thus forming a tertiary benzylic radical, would therefore stabilize this intermediate. 
However, it would also create steric interactions, when the cyclization step (7d → 7e) occurs. Once 7e has 
been formed, it spontaneously collapses into a carbonyl (aldehyde or ketone), and the liberated fragment. 
The photolytic release of amines from ortho-nitrobenzyl-derivered carbamates, and the influence of benzylic 
substituents have been investigated by Camron et al. [115].  
 
Figure 25  Reaction mechanism for photo cleavage. X = O, NH or S. R = H or Me. 
It was found that only a slight decrease in in quantum yield was obtained, when having a methyl group on 
the benzylic position (φ = 0.11 and φ = 0.13, for α-methyl and unsubstituted respectively). An imine formation 
==PAGE:46==
32 
 
could however not be ruled out as an unwanted side product, upon photolytic cleavage of an amine, and this 
effect is somewhat larger when releasing an aldehyde, thus favouring the α-methyl substituted complex 
when releasing amines.  
The synthesis of the photolinker construct was performed according to known procedure [116]. 10 (Scheme 
3) was formed by substituting commercial available acetovanilone (8) with ethyl 4-bromobutanoate (9) under 
basic conditions. Upon full conversion, the product was obtained after an extraction and a thorough wash 
with water, to ensure no traces of DMF. 11 was formed from a nitration of 10 by an electrophilic aromatic 
substitution. The electrophile is believed to be the in situ formed acetyl nitrate [117], and it has been shown 
in the literature, that the substitution occurs regioselectively at the 2-position under thermally controlled 
conditions. The most activating substituent in the aromatic complex is the methoxy-group, leading to ortho- 
and para-substitutions, and the substitution occurs at the least hindered position, which in this case is para 
to the methoxy-substituent. Continuous cooling of the reaction was proven difficult when scaling up the 
reaction, thus leading to a greater amount of disubstitution. Furthermore, the product needed to be filtered 
immediately after the reaction was quenched with an ice-water mixture to prevent hydrolysis of the ester. 
Hydrolysis did not seem to occur during the reaction, which most presumably is due to drain of the aqueous 
content of the concentrated HNO3, by an acid catalysed hydrolysis of the acetic anhydride, resulting in the 
formation of acetic acid.  
 
 
As it will be described later, the most successful coupling of the AMC-coupled lysine derivative to the 
photolinker was through a reductive alkylation using the ketone 11. The chloride (13) was however 
synthesized since it had previous been shown, that nucleophilic substitution with the primary α-amine of 
Scheme 2  Synthetic route and conditions for the different photolabile builind blocks. 
==PAGE:47==
33 
 
glycine gave the best results for these types of couplings (data not published). The chloride 13 was obtained 
by a reduction of 11 followed by a chlorination using thionyl chloride.  
The ketone (11) was chemoselectively reduced, using NaBH4 as a reducing agent. The reaction was quenched 
with saturated NH4Cl to hinder the hydroxide formation, thus preventing hydrolysis of the ester. 
Elimination was a concern, during the chlorination. The formed conjugated double bond seemed 
unavoidable, which hampered the purification of the product using flash column chromatography. The best 
eluent system for the purification was found to be CH2Cl2/Tol (7:3, v/v), but several overlapping fragments 
was obtained, and further purification was needed for obtaining a higher yield.   
 
2.1.2 Synthesis of Boc-Lys-AMC  
As mentioned earlier, the AMC-lysine derivative is the core of the target substrates, and is essential for 
screening and detecting the HDAC enzymes specific activity towards the peptide fragments. Coupling of 7-
amino-4-methylcoumarin (15, Scheme 3) to the carboxyl end of a protected lysine using phosphoryl chloride 
and pyridine, is a standard coupling procedure for these substrates and was first introduced by Jung et al. in 
1999 [33]. HATU coupling procedures have also been carried out, but showed poorer yields than the former 
described method [36]. The reason for this is the reactivity of the aniline amine, which is less nucleophilic 
than the α-amine of an amino acid, thus requiring a stronger activation agent such as POCl3 to complete the 
coupling. The method was first develop for coupling with even less reactive para-nitroanilines, and the acid 
activation is believed to go through an in situ acid chloride or phosphorous mixed anhydride formation [118]. 
The reaction was performed smoothly and 16 was obtained in quantitative yields, and was purified by an acid 
base extraction. 
Fmoc-Lys(Boc)-OH is the most commonly used lysine amino acid for solid phase peptide synthesis, but 
reviewing the synthetic strategy, the α-N protecting group should not be base-labile, due to complications 
with the hydrolysis step of the linker construct. Having an acid-labile Boc protecting group at the α-N position 
seemed more beneficial for the design of the construct, and is easily removed by standard conditions on solid 
support. The commercial available Boc-Lys(Fmoc)-OH (14) was chosen as the starting material for the 
synthesis, because it was beneficial price-wise, and met our criteria for an easy and selective removal of the 
ε-N protecting group, thus creating a good foundation for a development of a cheap and effective screening 
method.  
==PAGE:48==
34 
 
 
Protecting groups are an inevitable synthetic tool for most organic chemists, and the field has been 
developed to an extinct where the protection/deprotection are performed in quantitative yields, thus 
minimizing any loss of product during these steps. For amine protection, carbamates have shown to be very 
advantageous due to easy removal and many compounds (especially in the amino acid field) are commercial 
available as their Fmoc, Boc, and Bzl moieties. Using solid phase peptide synthesis, the Fmoc-strategy is used 
as standard coupling procedures today, and the α-N protecting group is usually cleaved by 20 vol% piperidine 
in DMF or NMP. These conditions are typically unfavourable in solution, due to low solvent volatility, and the 
scavenging of dibenzoefulvene (DBF) (18c → 18d, Figure 26) are depended on the piperidine concentration, 
and are solvent dependent. As an alternative, the non-nucleophilic base 1,8-Diazabicyclo[5.4.0]undec-7-ene 
(DBU) has shown to be a useful substitute for piperidine in these reactions [119]. The advantageous of using 
DBU is that it does not scavange DBF, thus only requiring a catalytic amount, and combining it with a solid 
supported thiol based scanvenger simplifies the purification of the product.  
 
For optimal reaction conditions for the Fmoc-deprotection of 14, a solvent screening of CH2Cl2, DMF, 1,4-
dioxane, EtOAc, and MeCN was performed. CH2Cl2 and dioxane showed a little to no conversion of the starting 
material within the first twenty minutes, judged by TLC. EtOAc and MeCN showed better results, but full 
conversion was only obtained in DMF. Based on those observations, DMF was chosen as the solvent for the 
reaction. This however caused the need for an extraction and a thorough wash with water to get rid of the 
Scheme 3 Synthetic route for the Boc-Lys-AMC building block obtained in two steps from commercially available Boc-Lys(Fmoc)-
OH and AMC. 
Figure 26    Mechanism for deprotection of the Fmoc protecting group. 
==PAGE:49==
35 
 
DMF. Complications was encountered due to slight water miscibility of the product, leading to loss of the 
product during this unit operation. The best results were obtained, when washing the organic phase five 
times with water, and then basify the combined water phases with KOH and then extracting it again with 
CH2Cl2.  
As an attempt to increase the yield of the Fmoc deprotection, MeCN was tested as the solvent. This simplified 
the work-up procedure by excluding the extraction, and upon full conversion of the starting material, the 
solvent was removed in vacuo and directly purified by flash column chromatography. The change of solvent 
prolonged the reaction time to 1.5 h upon full conversion, but NMR analysis showed that among minor 
impurities, approximately 1 equiv. of piperidine was still present in the sample, which verified that the 
extraction step could not be excluded.  
 
2.1.3 Coupling reaction  
Coupling the two synthesized building blocks and exploiting the advantages of a convergent synthetic 
strategy, seemed to be the most difficult step in the synthesis of the linker construct. As can be seen in Table 
2, several reaction conditions have been carried out to optimize the product formation in highest possible 
yields.  
As mentioned earlier, it has previous been shown that nucleophilic substitution on the chloride complex (13) 
gave the best result, when coupling to these linker constructs with primary amines (non-published results). 
These types of reactions have proven to be quite slow, so to enhance the reaction rate a catalytic amount of 
potassium iodide was added (Entry 1, Table 2). The iodine is then introduced to the complex via a halide 
exchange in an equilibrium with the chloride. The negative charge on the iodine molecule are spread out on 
a larger surface area, thus giving a more diffuse electron cloud, which facilitate a more effective orbital 
overlap in the transition state due to a lower energy barrier and thereby increasing the reaction rate.  A small 
amount of product was formed after 20 h of stirring at 60 oC, but several other side products were 
predominantly formed including the styrene derived elimination product.   
Entry 2 was carried out using the alcohol (12) under Mitsunobu reaction conditions. These conditions lead 
quite rapidly to full conversion of the 12 to the undesired elimination side product. Conversion of substituted 
benzyl alcohols to amines using Mitsunobu reaction conditions have previous been reported in the literature 
[120]. It is suggested that a phosphonium intermediate leaves the complex and forms the triphenylphosphine 
oxide and a bezylic carbocation, which is stabilized by having an electron donation substituent ortho or para 
to the former benzyl alcohol, before the nucleophilic attack occurs. Given the basic conditions, the formation 
==PAGE:50==
36

==PAGE:51==
37 
 
styrene derivative from the presumably formed benzylic carbocation, were favoured over the desired 
substitution of the lysine ε-amine. 
Elimination has been an unavoidable side reaction in both formation and during coupling procedures of the 
alcohol (12) and chloride (13). To avoid this, the ketone(11) and amine (17) was coupled by reductive 
alkylation. Lewis acids are typically required for these types of reactions to activate the ketone, and 
commonly used lewis acids are either TiCl4 or Ti(O-iPr)4, depending on the substrate. Along with this is added 
a reducing agents such as NaBH3CN, NaBH(OAc)3 or NaBH4 [121, 122]. The advantages of using the two former 
reducing agents, is that they can be added to the reaction mixture along with the ketone, since they reduce 
the imine selectively, thus shifting the equilibrium towards the product amine. Contrary, NaBH4 is a stronger 
reducing agent and it is typically added upon full conversion of the ketone. The equilibrium can also be shifted 
by removing the formed water from the reaction, either by equipping the reaction setup with a Dean-Strak 
apparatus and reflux the immiscible solvent along with the water, or adding in activated molecular sieves (2 
– 3 Å, depending on the solvent).  
TiCl4 was the first tested lewis acid for the reductive alkylation. When adding the NaBH3CN in the beginning 
of the reaction, along with the starting material and TiCl4, the ketone was immediately reduced to the 
corresponding alcohol. TiCl4 seems to activate the ketone to an extinct where the otherwise non-reactive 
NaBH3CN could reduce the complex (Entry 3). To avoid this undesired reduction, TiCl4 was added in a new 
reaction to the starting material without any reducing agent, to see if full imine formation could be obtained 
(Entry 4). LC-MS analysis of the reaction mixture showed a great amount of Boc deprotection at these 
conditions. This can either be due to an in situ HCl formation from the TiCl4, or a lewis acid mediated 
deprotection. 
In Entry 5 the weaker Ti(O-iPr)4 lewis acid was tested under similar conditions as in Entry 3, and the result 
from that reaction was much more promising than any of the former. The Lewis acid was strong enough to 
activate the ketone, but not so strong that it lead to reduction, as it did with TiCl4. Both the imine and the 
product amine (19) were formed in a steady rate, and complete conversion of the starting material was 
observed within 6 h. The imine intermediate and the product could not be separated by neither TLC, HPLC, 
nor LC-MS, so it was difficult to estimate the amount of imine present in the reaction mixture. However, the 
work-up procedure and purification were performed after 21 h, although the imine [M+H]+ mass was still 
observed by LC-MS. A more complex reaction mixture occurred over time, including a side product with a 
[M+H+CH3]+ mass. A logical explanation for this phenomenon is, that a transesterification occurred between 
the Ti(O-iPr)4 and the ethyl ester. From that reaction, 35% yield was isolated by flash column chromatography, 
and it was discovered that a significant amount of imine was not reduced. The formed imine is in conjugation 
==PAGE:52==
38 
 
with the aromatic system and the nucleophilic addition of the reducing agent hydride is sterically hindered, 
thus hampering the product formation.   
To avoid the transesterification side product, Ti(OEt)4 was used as the lewis acid, as shown in entries 6, 7, 
and 8. The properties of this Lewis acid was similar to Ti(O-iPr)4, and full conversion of the starting material 
was obtained within 6 h. Upon full conversion, the stronger reducing agent NaBH4 was added in an EtOH 
solution to fully reduce the imine. When the reaction was left over-night (Entry 6), the imine was fully 
reduced. However, several side products started to appear under these conditions, including cleavage of the 
AMC group. It seems that full reduction could not be achieved, without any significant side product 
formation, and the ratio between these two parameters was difficult to estimate. Using the same procedure 
as in entry 6, but with a shorter reaction time of NaBH4, fewer side products were formed, which also 
increased the yield (entry 7). However, it seemed that the presence of the Lewis acid along with the NaBH4 
reducing agent was the main reason for the drop in yield over time. Therefore, in an attempt to further 
optimize the reaction, the Lewis acid was quenched by addition of water upon full conversion of the starting 
material, and a residue containing the imine was isolated by extraction and the solvent was removed in vacuo 
(entry 8). This residue was then dissolved in the protic solvent MeOH, which allowed the reduction to occur 
more rapidly at a much lower temperature. Upon addition of the reducing agent, the reaction mixture 
immediately changed colour from orange-brown to light yellow. Due to the more rapid reduction at lower 
temperatures without the lewis acid present, less side products were obtained, and the target amine (19) 
was isolated in 75% yield. 
The advantage of coupling the two building blocks through a reductive alkylation, is that it saves two synthetic 
steps in the overall synthesis, thus giving a more elegant and efficient synthetic strategy. As shown in the 
reaction scheme, two diastereomers are formed in the reaction in equal amounts as judged by the intensity 
of the peaks in the 1H NMR spectra (see Appendix A).  
The formed stereocenter is however not an issue in the overall synthesis, since this bond is photolytically 
cleaved upon irradiation in later steps (see retrosynthetic analysis Scheme 1).  
 
2.1.4 Ester hydrolysis  
To be able to couple the linker construct to the solid support, the photolinker ester needed to be hydrolysed 
to obtain the corresponding acid. This was obtained as shown in Scheme 4 under basic conditions 
==PAGE:53==
39 
 
using LiOH in a THF/H2O mixture. Using five equivalents of LiOH, the product formation occurred in a steady 
rate, and full conversion of the starting material was obtained after 12 h of stirring at ambient temperature.  
An unexpected side product was observed under these conditions, and the mass correspond to the hydrolysis 
and ring-opening of the coumarin ester was observed. As shown in Table 3, these two compounds were 
formed in an equal distribution after 12 h of stirring and continuous stirring for an additional 8 h, did not shift

2.2 Library development 
To establish the best criteria for identification of highly active substrates in a bead based assay screen, some 
pre-solution based tests and general considerations needed to be carried out, prior to developing the OBOC 
library. Since the goal for the hit-identification was not settled as an attempt to identify a general PTM or 
peptide sequences corresponding to a specific recognition site, the library design was not restricted by any 
specific acylations or usage of natural occurring amino acids. As mentioned in section 2.1, there are two 
handles on the linker construct that can be modified selectively. It was estimated that the library should 
contain approximately 3–5 different acylations and di-, tri-, and tetra-peptide chains from 20–30 different 
amino acids, which would ultimately give a library of 25.000 – 80.000 different compounds. As mentioned in 
section 1.5, variations on the N-terminal side of an ε-N acetylated lysine have been conducted. By having the 
ability to create large diversities on this handle (R2, see Table 4), no further effort was gone into investigating 
this area of the library development. The variations of the acylations (R1, see Table 4), were on the other 
hand more limited, and only highly active or selective acylations were desirable. In addition, the reported 
data concerning the activity of different acylations towards the individual isoforms are limited. Therefore, a 
number of substrates with varying acylations were synthesized and screened against all 11 Zink-dependent 
isoforms for their respectively deacylase activity to identify highly active acylations, that could be used in the 
library development. As shown in Table 4, compounds with 15 different acylations have been synthesized, 
with three different variations on the R2-position. The Boc-protected compounds were generated to find and 
optimize the reaction conditions that were required to modify the R1-position, and to verify the structure by 
NMR-analysis. After identifying suitable reaction conditions, the acylations were performed on the complex 
having an Ac-Leu-Gly on the R2-position. The Leu-Gly-Lys(Ac) is based on the amino acid sequence 10-12 of 
core histone H4, and has previously shown great activity among many of the HDAC isoforms [45, 42].  
As will be described in the following section, no general acylation procedure for establishing the shown R1-
modifications could be found, and each single acylation had to be carefully optimized to ensure a full and 
clean conversion. Therefore, a setup was required, where the construct could easily be cleaved from the solid 
support and analysed by LC-MS. As mentioned in section 1.6.2, the HMBA linker is cleaved by hydrolysis of 
the benzyl ester, thus giving the corresponding acid. The quantity required for a LC-MS analysis could be 
obtained after cleaving 1-2 mg of resin for 15 min in a 0.1 M NaOH solution, which made the HMBA linker an 
obvious choice for anchoring the photolinker during the preparation of these test-substrates. The HMBA 
linker was coupled to a PEGA resin, using TBTU coupling conditions (see section 5.3).

The disadvantage of using a HMBA linker is that an ester bond is formed upon coupling with the first acid 
residue. Ester formations are typically less reactive than their respectively amide counterparts, and two 
coupling procedures are required with 3 equivalents of acid when using the coupling reagent MSNT [123], as 
shown in Scheme 5. Therefore, to reduce the required amount of synthesized photolinker, the first coupling 
was performed with the Fmoc-protected phenylalanine. Coupling with MSNT has shown to give a better 
conversion than carbodiimides such as DCC, or HOBt active esters. However, to avoid racemization, only 2.3 
equivalents of base are recommended for these coupling reactions, which hampers the reaction rate. 
 
After coupling of the phenylalanine and deprotection of the Fmoc-group, a free amine was present on the 
solid support and full coupling of the photolinker construct could be accomplished by using only 1.1 
equivalents under HATU coupling conditions, (see Scheme 6). In additions, cleavage from the HMBA linker 
indicated that no disubstitution on the secondary amine occurred under these conditions, which most 
presumably is due to the low reactivity of this amine. This meant, that no protection of this amine was 
required, but as will be discussed in the following section, it also meant that quite harsh conditions were 
required to ensure full acylation at that position.  
 
Scheme 5 Ester coupling on HMBA functionalized PEGA resin using MSNT coupling reagent. As indicated, the coupling was 
proceeded twice. Structure of MSNT is displayed in dashed box.  
Scheme 6 Coupling of the synthesized photolinker to solid support using HATU coupling reagent. Note that 
the primary amine displayed on the solid support is the α-N of Phe obtained in scheme 5.  The 
resin functionalization is the same throughout this section, and will not be presented in the 
following schemes.  
==PAGE:57==
43 
 
 
The Ac-Leu-Gly derivatives were prepared by a Boc-deprotection in TFA/CH2Cl2 (1:1, v/v), followed by 
standard TBTU couplings with Fmoc-Gly-OH and Ac-Leu-OH (Scheme 7B ). The latter was prepared in solution 
by simultaneously addition of acetic anhydride and NaOH as shown in Scheme 7A [124].  It was found that 
polymerization of the leucine occurred when using acidic conditions. This is most presumably due to 
activation of the acid carbonyl and acid catalysed hydrolysis of the acetic anhydride. 
 
2.2.1 Synthesis of the ε-N acylations 
The aim for the scope of acyl-variations on the secondary amine was to create modifications with both 
different sizes and electronic effects. As mentioned in section 1.4.2, the Ktfa has shown great activity towards 
the class IIa enzymes, which are typically not active towards standard ε-N acetylated peptides. However, it 
has been shown that this tendency is not due to lack of binding to these acetylated substrates in the class IIa 
enzymes. In vitro inhibition studies have actually shown that the class IIa isoforms binds the ε-N acetylated 
substrates with comparable affinity to those in class I and IIb, but fail to pursuit the enzymatic cleavage [45].  
In addition, having shown that these quite bulky Ktfa moieties can be ducked into the active site of the 
enzymes, it enabled one to investigate other relative bulky ε-N acylations with election withdrawing effects, 
to see if a borderline in activity could be reached.  
Three different types of reagents were used to form these different acylations, including anhydrides, acid 
chlorides, and acids which were pre-activated by triphosgene. Activated esters formed from acids with 
Scheme 7 Synthesis of the solid bound ground-structure for the Ac-Gly-Leu derivatives. A) Solution-based
synthesis of Ac-Leu-OH. B) Standard coupling and deprotection conditions for the synthesis of the solid 
bound Ac-Gly-Leu derivative. 
==PAGE:58==
44 
 
coupling reagents such as TBTU, HATU, PyBop, or Mukaiyamas reagent did not show any reactivity towards 
this secondary amine. All acylation procedures were carefully optimised in terms of usage of solvent, base, 
temperature, and reaction time, to ensure full coupling with a least amount of undesired side products. This 
was important when considering these modifications as a part of the OBOC library development, since any 
side product formation or non-completed reaction, would generate a cascade of uncertainties and 
hampering the analysis of the “hit” beads.  
 
2.2.2 Acylation using anhydrides 
For being able to verify the activity that are being gained from the pre-solution based screen, some reference 
compounds needed to be created to insure a comparable activity profile among the individual HDAC 
isoforms. An obvious choice for the class I and class IIb isoforms, was the ε-N acetylated lysine derivative 
(22b). Therefore, the first modification that was performed on the lysine ε-N, was an acetylation using acetic 
anhydride in DMF along with pyridine. Acetylations on a similar scaffold have previous been performed in 
solution by using 2 equiv. of Ac2O and pyridine in DMF [125]. These conditions did however not lead to any 
significant product formation on solid support, and the amount of reagents were therefore raised to see if 
the reaction could be pushed towards completion. Using 40 equiv. of acetic anhydride, 62% product 
formation was obtained after the first 5 h of shaking at rt. and after 12 h full conversion was obtained.  
 
These conditions could also be used for the very similar propionic anhydride to yield the products with high 
purity, as shown in Scheme 8. A general trend for all the photo-cleavages performed in this project is the low 
yields, which was typically around 25%. A reason for this could be as previously mentioned, the photolytically 
release from solid support are typically lower than similar experiments in solutions, due to light scattering 
and shadow effects. Furthermore, the loading of the PEGA resin is according to the supplier 0.4 mmol/g, but 
previously performed loading experiments have confirmed that the actual loading is closer to 0.3 mmol/g. 
Scheme 8 Acylation of the lysine ε-N using aliphatic anhydrides. R1 = Me or Et. R = NHBoc (a), Ac-Leu-Gly (b), 
and Ac-Gly (c) 
==PAGE:59==
45 
 
 
 
Using similar reaction conditions as in Scheme 8, it was shown that for the acylation using the 2-chloroacetic 
anhydride (Scheme 9), pyridine was substituted on approximately 16% of the product (judged by LC-MS). 
Pyridine is not a particular good nucleophile, but the chloride on the acetyl α-position is a very good leaving 
group, thus giving rise to the observed tendency. Therefore, a range of non-nucleophilic bases was tested to 
see if a cleaner conversion could be obtained, including DIPEA, Et3N, Proton-sponge® (1,8-
Bis(dimethylamino)naphthalene), and 2,4,6-collidine. All the tested reactions led to full conversion of the 
starting material, however for the three former mentioned bases, a complex reaction mixture was observed 
including 20-23% of the corresponding α-hydroxy derivative. The best result was obtained when using 2,4,6-
collidine as base for the reaction, thus leading to full conversion of the starting material with more than 95% 
purity, as shown in Scheme 9. In addition, the observed tendency can be due to a higher water content in 
the three former mentioned bases, and it should also be mentioned that no hydroxide substitution was 
observed during HMBA cleavage using 0.1 M NaOH.  
 
The solid bound chloride complex was used as a precursor for further modifications and introduction of other 
functional groups, such as the azide derivatives 33a and 33b shown in Scheme 10. Similar conditions were 
tested with KCN, but no substitution could be observed when using the cyanide anion as nucleophile.  
 
Scheme 9 Acylation of (38a, b) using 2-chloroacetic anhydride and the less nucleophilic base 2,4,6-collidine. R = NHBoc (a), Ac-
Leu-Gly (b). 
Scheme 10 Azide nucleophilic substitution on the chloride complex. R = NHBoc (a), Ac-
Leu-Gly (b). 
==PAGE:60==
46 
 
2.2.3 Trifluoroacetylation  
The final anhydride that has been used to modify the secondary ε-N, was trifluoroacetic anhydride (TFAA). 
The Ktfa derivative was not necessary a desirable acyl modification for the OBOC library due to its distinct 
deacetylase pattern, but it was an important reference compound for the solution based screen. Great effort 
have gone into finding reaction conditions that enabled the highly reactive TFAA species to selectively modify 
the complex, without disrupting or destroying the resin beads.  
Among several tested reaction conditions, it was found that product formation was observed when using 
either pyridine, 2,6-lutidine, or 2,4,6-collidine as bases in DMF, or Et3N, or DIPEA as bases in CH2Cl2. A 
disturbing pattern that was observed for all the tested reaction conditions was, that the reaction conditions 
which led to a positive outcomes was typically difficult to reproduce, especially when scaling up the reaction. 
Some general tendencies that hampered the reproducibility: 
 
The reaction was highly depending on the functionalities of the substrate that was coupled to the 
solid support. For example, reaction conditions that gave a generally good outcome on the α-N Boc-
protected residue (38a) could typically not be applied to the Ac-Leu-Gly (38b) complex. 
 
The solid support was degraded when the reaction period exceeded 1 h at rt. 
 
Within the time slot the resin was stable, full conversion of the starting material could not be 
accomplished. This meant that several reaction repetitions were required, which often led to 
complex mixtures.  
 
Positive outcomes seemed to be depending on the temperature of the reaction mixture upon 
addition of the reactants, the swelling of the beads, and the order of which the reactants was added. 
In total, more than 30 reactions have been carried out, as an attempt to find optimal reaction conditions that 
led to as few side products as possible, which could be scaled up, reproduced, and performed on substrates 
with different functionalities. Unfortunately, such conditions could not be found within the time frame of 
this project, and only two of the reactions led to full conversion of the starting material with no severe side 
product formation. The procedure for these reactions was based on a mixture of TFAA, Et3N, in CH2Cl2, and 
TFAA, pyridine, in DMF. The beads were swelled in the solvent, and in a separate vial was TFAA dissolved and 
cooled to -78 oC, and 0 oC respectively prior to the addition of the base. This solution was then added to the 
pre-swollen beads. However, neither of these reactions could be reproduced. 
 
==PAGE:61==
47 
 
 
For obtaining a sufficient amount of compound for some solution based assays, the trifluoroacetylated 
substrate (34a) was obtained in solution on the α-N Boc-protected lysine (17), as shown in Scheme 11. These 
assays will be discussed in section 4.  
 
2.2.4 Acylation using acid chlorides 
Several acylation has been performed using acid chlorides. A general trend for these reactions is that neither 
of them proceeded in DMF as opposed to the anhydrides, and much better results were obtained when using 
CH2Cl2 instead. Due to the high reactivity of the acid chlorides, they were very sensitive to what type of base 
that was used in each reaction.  
 
 
 
Benzoyl chloride was the first acid chloride to be tested, and the conditions for the reaction were an adjusted 
procedure from previous performed solid supported benzoylations on secondary amines, where the resin 
was suspended in benzoyl chloride/CH2Cl2 (1:1, v/v) along with 1 equiv. of pyridine [126]. However, to ensure 
that no HCl formation from a potential hydrolysis of the acid chloride occured, thus leading to cleavage of 
the Boc-group, an excess of base was added (see Scheme 12). After 16 h of shaking under these conditions, 
only 50% of the starting material was converted to the desired product. The benzoylation reaction was 
therefore repeated one additional time, which led to formation of several side products, and only 81% purity 
of the crude product could be obtained. A cleaner conversion was obtained when activating benzoic acid 
with triphosgene (see section 2.2.5). 
Scheme 12 
Acylation of the ε-N using benzoyl chloride. Optimized reaction conditions was performed using BTC 
on benzoic acid, see section 2.2.5. 
Scheme 11  Reaction scheme for trifuoroactylation of Boc-Lys-AMC in solution  
==PAGE:62==
48 
 
 
 
The crotonylation on the Ac-Leu-Gly residue was another reference compound [42]. The ε-N crotonyl (Kcr) is 
a general accepted PTM of histones, and has been identified by mass spectrometry on 28 different sites [127]. 
As an attempt to adapt the conditions that have been used to benzoylate the lysine ε-amine, it was found 
that rapidly after addition of the pyridine a red oily substance was formed, thus leading to no conversion of 
the starting material. Similar observations were found when using Et3N, or DIPEA, but when using 1,8-
Diazabicycloundec-7-ene (DBU) at 0 oC, no red oily formation was observed, and the crotonylation was 
proceeded after 8 h of shaking and led to product formation with high purity, as shown in Scheme 13.  
 
 
Another conjugated modification was introduced by reacting the lysine-linker construct with acryloyl 
chloride. This reagent was discovered to be even more reactive and base sensitive than the crotonyl chloride. 
A black oily substance was obtained after addition of pyridine, Et3N, or DIPEA at 0 oC. Therefore, DBU was 
tested to see if similar results could be obtained, as for the crotonylation. This reaction was prepared by 
swelling the resin in CH2Cl2, and in a separate vial was acryloyl chloride dissolved in CH2Cl2 and cooled to 0 oC 
before the addition of the DBU. The mixture was then shaken for 5 min before being added to the pre-swollen 
resin. However, at these conditions three major products were formed with mass’ that correlate to the 
suggested structures that has been shown in Figure 27. 
The basis for these suggested structures rely on the unexpected high mass of 40a and 40b, which indicates 
that DBU is a part of the overall structure. Both of the structures rely on a Michael-addition on the acryloyl 
chloride. Assuming that the ε-N lysine moiety reacts by a nucleophilic attack on the Michael acceptor of the 
acryloyl chloride, having chloride as a leaving group, it would form a ketene. The highly reactive ketene can 
Scheme 13   Acylation of the ε-N using crotonyl chloride. R = NHBoc (a), Ac-Leu-Gly (b). 
Scheme 14    Acylation of the ε-N using acryloyl chloride 
==PAGE:63==
49 
 
then either react with the imine of DBU in a [2+2] cycloaddition forming a four membered β-lactam ring, or 
be hydrolysed by any water present in the mixture, thus giving suggested structures (40a and 41a). The other 
possible route that could give rise to the observed mass’, would be if the acryloyl chloride was substituted 
by a 1,2-addition forming the desired product (42), which then could react from the 1,4-addition with DBU, 
thus giving the charged iminium species (40b). The likeliness of which route that would lead to the suggested 
compounds is difficult to estimate, but DBU is considered to be a non-nucleophilic base, and no other 
observations of Michael additions of the acrylamide has been observed.  
To confirm the outcome of this reaction would require further investigation and analysis, which was not 
prioritized during this project. 
 
 
 
Since pure product formation could not be obtained with any of the tested non-nucleophilic bases at 0 oC, 
the reactivity of the acid chloride was tested at lower temperatures. It was found that the acryloyl chloride 
was stable towards addition of the DIPEA at -78 oC, and after allowing the mixture to reach ambient 
temperature while shaking the mixture for 1.5 h, full conversion of the starting material was obtained with a 
high purity as shown in Scheme 14. 
Figure 27 Suggested structures, whose mass correspond to found mass from the chromatogram (ESI). The compounds 
were released from the solid support by cleavage of the HMBA linker using 0.1 M NaOH. The percentage shown 
under each suggested structure, correlates to the distribution of the individual isomer in the mixture.  
==PAGE:64==
50 
 
 
 
The final lysine ε-N modification that was carried out for the solution based assay using an acyl chloride, was 
the cyclopropane (26). This modification was easier to control than the two former described, and full 
conversion with a high purity was obtained after 12 h of shaking at ambient temperature, using Et3N as base 
(see Scheme 15). 
 
2.2.5 Acylation using BTC 
Bis(trichloromethyl) carbonate (BTC) is a reagent that is used to form the highly toxic phosgene in situ. The 
uses of BTC in SPPS have been to create acid chlorides in situ, as an activation agent during coupling 
procedures, as mentioned in section 1.6.3. BTC coupling procedures have shown its application on general 
building blocks for the Fmoc strategy, including residues containing acid labile side chain protection groups. 
The generation of these in situ formed acid chloride has shown to be especially useful for couplings with 
sterically hindered amines, such as secondary amines. In addition, studies have shown that acid chlorides, 
which have been pre-prepared from SOCl2 are less reactive than those generated from triphosgene [128].  
 
The BTC coupling procedure that has been used in this project, is a slightly modified protocol from the one 
that has been developed by Jung et al. [129]. In the procedure, two different bases are used for pre-activation 
Scheme 15     Acylation of the ε-N using cyclopropanecarbonyl chloride. R = NHBoc (a), Ac-Leu-Gly (b). 
Figure 28 Illustration of the effects of a weaker base during the activation step. A weaker base-proton bond 
will result in a higher degree of phosgene carbonyl activation, thus accelerating the rate-
determining step in the formation of the acid chloride. 
==PAGE:65==
51 
 
and for coupling respectively. For the coupling, the strongest of the two bases (DIPEA) was used, whereas 
during activation the weaker 2,4,6-collidine was used. Weaker bases have shown to accelerate the activation 
step, since both the free acid and its corresponding anion are important when determining the overall rate 
of the conversion of the acid [130]. The effect of the “weak” base is that it deprotonates the acid, thus 
promoting the attack from the carboxylate without unduly interfering with the first step of protonating the 
phosgene carbonyl oxygen. A weaker base-proton bond will enable a higher degree of phosgene carbonyl 
activation, which facilitates the relative weak acid nucleophile to perform an attack and forming the mixed 
anhydride, as shown in Figure 28. However, it was necessary to have DIPEA in the reaction mixture, since it 
was found that the 2,4,6-collidine was not basic enough to prevent the formed HCl during the activation step 
from interfering and cleave some acid labile protection groups and linkers (primarily trityl based).

this compound in an aqueous media. c Coupled as their Fmoc protected derivative, which was cleaved by 
standard procedure on solid support. d Two coupling procedures was required for full conversion. e A 
complex mixture was observed when irradiating this compound for longer periods.    
==PAGE:66==
52 
 
 
Jung et al. described in their procedure, that they were able to obtain full coupling to resin bound secondary 
amines when using 5 equiv. of acid and 1.65 equiv. of BTC, but when applying these conditions to this setup, 
only 50% conversion was observed within the first 3 h. When raising the amount of acid and BTC to 10 and 
3.3 equiv. respectively, full conversion was generally observed for these couplings within 3 h. All the 
modifications was proceeded by the same general procedure, however the Fmoc-protected amino acids 
glycine and β-alanine required two couplings for going to completion (see entries 5 and 6 Table 5). In 
addition, it was found that when cleaving compounds with a free amine for longer periods than 30 min, 
severe side product formation started to appear. Therefore, the cleavage procedure for these compounds 
was repeated four times to afford high yield and reducing the side product formation.  
 
2.2.6 Steric complications and non-reactive species  
The low reactivity of the secondary ε-amine hampered the scope of modifications that could be applied to 
this position.  
 
The isobutyl group seemed to be the borderline of modifications that could be applied to the solid bound 
scaffold, but the procedure needed to be performed three times for 12 h before full conversion was 
observed, and several side products started to appear at these conditions. Product formation could only be 
obtained on the α-N Boc-protected compound (see Scheme 16). In addition, no sight of product formation 
could be found when the same conditions were used with the trimethyl acetyl chloride.   
As shown in Table 5 (entry 2), optimized conditions were obtained when using BTC conditions for the 
benzoylation, in terms of reaction-time and purity, compared with the method using benzoyl chloride (see 
Scheme 12). The same was therefore tested with isobutyric acid, to see whether this could improve the yield 
Scheme 16    Acylation of ε-N amine using isobutyryl chloride. The procedure was performed three times, which 
resulted in low purity of the crude product. 
==PAGE:67==
53 
 
and purity under milder reactions conditions. Unfortunately, this was not the case and despite the general 
utility of this method, several other acids showed similar observation (see Figure 29).  
 
A general trend for the small acid residues that contains slightly acidic protons (propiolic acid, cyanoacetic 
acid, and pyruvic acid), could be that upon activation of the acid to the corresponding acid chloride, the 
compound can polymerize rather than react with the solid bound secondary amine. Thus, leading to no 
product formation when performing the BTC coupling. 
 
2.3 Synthetic strategy for the in-bead HTS library 
Once a wide range of acylations were carried out, thus giving the ability to selectively modify the lysine ε-N, 
the next step in the process was to adjust the second handle of the linker construct, which included α-N 
peptide elongation. The former solid phase construct, consisted of a phenylalanine anchored to the solid 
Figure 29 Compounds which did not lead to product formation when 
using the BTC coupling procedure. 
Figure 30 Illustration of the synthetic strategy for the in-bead HTS. The setup has two cleavable positions; a photolabile, which is 
used prior to the in-bead assay screen, and an acid labile, which is used for post-screening analysis. The complex is added 
two additional moieties, which include a 4-bormophenylalanine, and an arginine moiety. These spacer units serves to
give a specific isotope pattern, and increasing the MS signal intensity.  
==PAGE:68==
54 
 
support by a HMBA linker. However, another design was required for the development of the OBOC in-bead 
HTS library, which would facilitate the single bead post-screening substrate analysis.  
As mentioned in section 1.7, one single bead contain approximately 100 pmol of compound, and for be able 
to perform a full sequence analysis of the attached compound, full cleavage is required. Even though cleavage 
of the HMBA linker typically is associated with high yields, the requisite of relatively large amounts of aqueous 
NaOH and HCl during cleavage, hampered the utility of this linker for single bead post-screening analysis. In 
contrast, the acid labile Rink amide linker is more applicable for this setup, since this linker can easily be 
cleaved in small volumes of the relative volatile acid TFA. The Rink amide linker was therefore chosen as an 
easy cleavable anchor for the PEGA solid support. Before coupling the synthesized lysine derived photolinker 
(20), two additional spacer units were added onto the complex. The first was the 4-bromophenylalanine 
moiety (see Figure 30). The [M+2] mass peak with an equal intensity is a specific isotope pattern for bromine-
containing structures, which enable one to readily identify the relevant peaks in the MS-analysis. The other 
spacer unit was a Pbf-protected arginine moiety (Ppf group is not shown in Figure 30), whose purpose is to 
facilitate ionization and produce a MS-signal of sufficient intensity. In addition, the two added spacer 
moieties serves to increase the total mass of the acid cleaved complex, which is favourable since most MALDI-
TOF instruments has a lower mass limit of approximately 300–500 Da. The Rink amide linker, the 4-
bromophenylalanine, and the Pbf-protected arginine were all coupled to the solid support as their Fmoc-
protected moieties by standard TBTU coupling procedure.  
  
Scheme 17 
Selective Boc-deprotection on an acid labile Rink amide linker. Full cleavage was obtained for compound 43 
(a-c), whereas no conversion was observed for compound 43 (d-f).  
==PAGE:69==
55 
 
When using the Rink amide linker, the α-N Boc-protecting 
group could not be cleaved by standard TFA/CH2Cl2 
conditions, due to complications with the acid lability of 
the linker construct. Therefore, an alternative procedure 
needed to be carried out, which could selectively 
deprotect the tert-butoxy carbamate protection group, 
without interfering with the Rink linker. Selective 
deprotections are of course highly desirable for an organic 
chemist, and great efforts have gone into developing such 
conditions. The usage of the TMSOTf and 2,6-lutidine have 
been described by Burgess et al. as conditions for selective 
removal of Boc-protecting groups on an acid labile solid 
support [131]. In their work, the deprotection was 
performed in 1 M TMSOTf and 1.5 M 2,6-lutidine. 
However, these conditions were not suitable for our setup and the anchored compound was cleaved from 
the solid support at these conditions. Therefore, different concentrations were tested to see if the conditions 
could be optimized. As shown in Figure 31, it was found that the borderline for cleavage of the Rink amide 
linker was between 0.4 M and 0.5 M TMSOTf in CH2Cl2 (along with 0.8 M and 1 M 2,4-lutidine respectively). 
The red colorization of the solution is caused by the corresponding carbocation, which is formed upon 
cleavage of the Rink linker. Similar observations have previously been stated by Bonnet et al. [132]. 
Additionally, it was found that this procedure could not be applied as a general procedure for Boc-
deprotection on all substrates, since the ability to cleave the Boc-group and the tolerance towards the 
TMSOTf, was highly depending on the other substituents of the overall complex, as shown in Scheme 17.  
 
 
Figure 31 Reaction mixtures with 0.4 M TMSOTf 
(yellow solution) and 0.5 M TMSOTf (red 
solution). Performed on substrate 43a. 
Figure 32    Mechanism for a Lewis acid promoted Boc-deprotection.  
==PAGE:70==
56 
 
It was found that those substrates with acylations that did not lead to full deprotection under 0.4 M TMSOTf, 
also were more tolerant towards higher concentrations of this Lewis acid. These substrates (43d, e, f) 
tolerated a TMSOTf concentrations up to 1 M before slight red colorization occurred, which also led to full 
deprotection. The mechanism for the process is believed to go through an activation of the carbamate 
carbonyl by the TMS, which pull the electrons through the complex and facilitates deprotonation and 
elimination of the tert-butyl group, forming isobutylene and the carbamic acid, as shown in Figure 32.  
 
2.3.1 Amino acids for the OBOC library 
When choosing the amino acids for the library synthesis, it was important to identify whether amino acids 
with side-chain protection groups could be present in the library, and what conditions were required for 
selectively removal of these protecting groups, without affecting the Rink amide linker. Since the 
deprotection step should be carried out as the last step of the library synthesis and this step could not be 
analysed due to the complex mixture of compounds in the library, the deprotection needed to be sufficient 
in very mild conditions that did not destroy or cleave any compounds in the mixture.  
To test this, the two amino acid residues Fmon-Glu(OtBu)-OH, and Fmoc-His(Boc)-OH was coupled to the 
linker construct and treated with 0.4 M TMSOTf and 0.8 M 2,6-lutidine (Scheme 18). These conditions did 
not lead to any deprotection, and due to the previous reviewed issues with higher concentrations of TMSOTf, 
no other conditions were tested. Another attempt to broaden the scope of side-chain protected amino acids, 
the deprotection of the trityl-protected residue Fmoc-Ser(Trt)-OH was treated with TFA/TIS/CH2Cl2 (2:2:96, 
v/v/v) for 2 x 15 min. These conditions led to cleavage of the trityl group, but minor impurities also started 
Scheme 18 
Attempts to selectively cleave the tert-butoxy protecting groups on Fmoc-His(Boc)-R (a) and Fmoc-Glu(OtBu)-R (b)
respectively using TMSOTf deprotection conditions. 45a, b was synthesized from 38a using acetylation conditions 
(Scheme 8), followed by Boc deprotection using TFA/CH2Cl2 (1:1, v/v), and then coupling the Fmoc-protected amino 
acid using TBTU (see section 5.3). 
==PAGE:71==
57 
 
to appear. In addition, other trityl based amino acids, such as Asn(Trt), and His(Trt) generally require higher 
concentrations of TFA, therefore these could not be a part of the library [133]. 
The selective deprotection of the acid labile side chain protection groups that are commercial available for 
amino acids to be used in the Fmoc/tBu strategy proved challenging. It was therefore decided that only amino 
acids that did not require further treatment, should be a part of the library.  
 
A full list of all the amino acids that have been used for the OBOC library can be found in appendix B. The aim 
was to investigate a large variety of aliphatic and aromatic residues, based on the previous performed 
profiling studies (see section 1.5). Due to the nature of the library development using “split-and-mix” 
synthesis, each coupling could not be analysed. Therefore, to ensure complete coupling, each residue was 
coupled two times with HATU coupling reagent for one hour. After each coupling round, an amount of beads 
equal to the overall distribution of compounds in the total library was put aside, so that equal distributions 
of both di-, tri-, and tetra-peptide chains were formed. In total 23 different amino acids were used for the 
split-and-mix synthesis, which including the five different acylations gave a library containing approximately 
63,000 different compounds (see Scheme 19, these five acylations will be discussed further in section 2.4). 
An important note is that all of the amino acids used for the library have different mass’. This is advantageous 
when performing the post-screening analysis (using MS/MS) and identifying the specific amino acid 
corresponding to a given mass-difference. After all three rounds of couplings, the α-N Fmoc protecting groups 
were removed and the formed primary amine was acetylated using a highly effective acetylation procedure, 
as shown in Scheme 20 [134].  
 
Scheme 19 
Illustration of OBOC library development using 5 different acylations (44b, c, d, e, f) and 23 different Fmoc-protected
amino acids. The HATU coupling procedure was repeated twice for each residue to ensure complete coupling. Three 
coupling rounds were performed and after each round, an amount of beads equal to the overall distribution of 
synthesized compounds were put aside. Thus, di-, tri-, and tetra-peptides were formed (n = 1, 2, and 3). 
==PAGE:72==
58 
 
 
This acetylation method has shown to be a superior method for amine acetylations on solid support. Typical 
procedures for these kinds of modification are performed with Ac2O under basic conditions, however that 
method has sometimes failed to provide adequate yields, especially on secondary amines, such as proline 
residues. After kinetic calculations and NMR analysis, a mechanism was suggested as shown in Figure 33 as 
being the most feasible process for acetylations at these conditions. Initially, the malonate reacts with the 
TBTU and –OBt leaves the complex. This intermediate then undergoes a self-dissociation, to form CO2 and 
the highly reactive ketene, which immediately reacts with the solid bound amine to form the acetyl group.  
 
 
Scheme 20   α-N acetylation of library beads. 
Figure 33     Suggested mechanism for amine acetylation using activated malonic acid. 
==PAGE:73==
59 
 
2.4 HDAC substrate solution based profiling 
To establish the best foundations for a bead based screen, a pre-solutions based screen was performed on 
all 14 different acylations of the Ac-Leu-Gly functionalized derivatives. The results obtained from these 
studies are presented in graphs, revealing the specific activity towards all 11 Zink-dependent HDAC enzymes 
(see Figure 34, graphs with logarithmical values can be found in appendix C). To compare the results obtained 
from this study, a single dose activity assay was carried out, using a 10 μM substrate concentration with 50 
ng recombinant enzyme in each well [45, 42]. The fixed concentrations of substrate and enzyme for this 
screen, does however not take into account the possible difference in concentrations required to reach 
optimal linearity for each enzyme. Three reference compounds were applied to the collection of tested 
substrates, which includes substrate 22b, 28b, and 34b. These three compounds showed comparable activity 
profiles to the previous reported, hence substrate 22b towards HDAC1, 2, 3, and 6, substrate 28b towards 
HDAC3 (low activity), and substrate 34b towards HDAC3, 4, 5, 7, and 8. The latter has however previous 
shown activity towards HDAC9, which was not the case for this screen [45, 42]. 
As shown in Figure 34, many of the tested substrates showed great deacylase activity among several of the 
recombinant HDAC enzymes. Despite being potential ε-N modifications for the in-bead OBOC library, other 
distinct patterns and trends were observed, which will be discussed in the following. 
A general, not so surprisingly trend was the high activity of HDAC1, 2, and 3 towards most of the active 
substrates at the low enzyme concentrations used for this screen. HDAC3 seemed to be the most active and 
tolerant towards steric capacity and electronic effects. Reviewing the steric tolerance of these three 
enzymes, it seemed as if the bulkiness of the acyl group has a larger impact on their ability to hydrolyse the 
substrates, rather than the length for HDAC1, and 2. These two enzymes were able to deacylate substrates 
such as 23b, 27b, 30b, 32b, and 33b, which contain straight and rotatable acyl moieties. However, when the 
substituent tend to increase and be more spatial demanding, these two enzymes lost their enzymatic activity. 
When replacing an oxygen atom in substrate 30b to a larger sulphur atom in substrate 31b, the enzymatic 
activity of HDAC1, and 2 decreased by 63% and 66% respectively, whereas HDAC3 only lost 14% of its activity 
compared to substrate 30b. In addition, the HDAC3 proved to be more than 4 times as active towards 
hydrolysing substrate 31b than HDAC2. In comparison, the difference in activity between these two enzymes, 
(in this setup) are approximately 15% for the acetylated substrates 22b and 22c. However, an important note 
is that the data obtained from this screen, only enable comparison of activity under these specific conditions. 
The concrete catalytic efficiencies of the different enzymes isoforms on various substrates require 
determination of kinetic parameters by performing Michaelis-Menten analysis.

Another spatial demanding acylation, which gave rise to HDAC3 isoform selectivity, can be found in 
compound 26b. This modification exhibited a 32-fold and 15-fold difference in catalytic activity to HDAC1 
and 2 respectively, and did not show any significant deacylase activity towards any of the other HDAC 
isoforms. This tendency has been observed in the development of Class I selective inhibitors, where 
derivatives containing the 2-aminoanilide functional group as the Zink-chelator part of the inhibitor, have 
shown great selectivity towards HDAC1, 2, and 3. The cause for this observed selectivity is due to the cavity 
around the active site of these three enzymes, which enable more bulky moieties to reach the Zink-binding 
domain, compared to HDAC8 and members of class II [135, 136]. This however slightly contradicts the 
observed activity of HDAC4, 5, 7, and 8 (typically also 9) towards the sterically demanding trifluoroacetyl 
group on substrate 34b, but this effect has been addressed as being caused by electronic effects, substrate 
association, and/or product release.  
A question arose when the selective activity of HDAC3 towards substrate 26b was found. Was the observed 
tendency due to a lack of substrate association in the active site of the other members of the class I enzymes, 
due to steric hindrance, or did the substrate bind to the active site Zink atom, but the enzymes failed to 
perform the hydrolysis of the amide bond? To answer this question, a dose-response assay were carried out 
as shown in Figure 35.  
The inhibitor assay was prepared by following a recommended procedure from commercial available 
fluorometric drug discovery kit, obtained from Enzo Life Science [137]. In the assay the two deacetylase 
substrates Ac-Arg-His-Lys-Lys(Ac)-AMC (final concentration of 15 μM) and Ac-Arg-His-Lys(Ac)-Lys(Ac)-AMC 
(final concentration of 25 μM) was used for HDAC1 and 8 respectively. Upon addition of the recombinant 
enzyme, the plates were incubated for 1 h prior to the development by addition of Trypsin. The results from

==PAGE:77==
63 
 
this screen state, despite having an approximately 7-fold and 4-fold excess of substrate 26b, that no 
competitive inhibition of the deacetylase activity could be observed for each of the two class I HDAC isoforms. 
From this screen, it can be argued that the observed lack of enzymatic activity among the class I enzymes 
HDAC1, and 2 (and 8) is primarily due to steric complications, which prevent the substrate from reaching the 
catalytic site. If a threshold for enzymatic steric capacity among the HDAC class I enzymes have been found, 
this knowledge can be used to develop new selective inhibitors for HDAC3, by adapting the cyclopropyl group 
into the Zink-binding domain of a potential inhibitor.  
As mentioned in section 1.4.1, no selective inhibitors for 
HDAC1 or 2 have been found yet, due to the great similarity 
of these two isoforms. In this screen, a tendency in the 
relative difference of catalytic activity was observed, when 
the length of the acyl chain was extended. The difference in 
catalytic activity between the two isoforms HDAC1 and 2, 
was 48% for the standard acetylated substrate 22b. This 
difference was then slightly enlarged for the ε-N propionyl 
lysine derivative substrate 23b to 56%. However, when the 
chain was further prolonged, as in the cases of substrate 
30b, 31b, and 33b, the difference in enzymatic activity rose 
to 69%, 67%, and 70% respectively. This indicates that the 
HDAC2 could have a slightly larger or longer foot pocket, than HDAC1. The foot pocket of the HDAC enzymes, 
is a small cavity positioned immediately adjacent to the active site.  The capacity of the foot-pocket has been 
investigated for HDACIs towards the class I enzymes, as shown in Figure 36 [59] (this also applies for substrate 
1, which was mentioned in section 1.4.1).   
An unexpected activity was found for HDAC6 towards the α-chloro substrate 32b. This isoform, which is a 
member of the class IIb enzymes, has not shown any activity towards other acyl moieties than the standard 
ε-N acetylated species. As shown in Figure 37, the enzyme loses its activity, when the acyl moiety is extended 
to the propionyl derivative 23b. This phenomenon has previously been described by Jung et al. on an α-N 
benzyl protected substrate [43]. However in this screen, slight activity was observed for the acrylamide 
(substrate 27b) and as mentioned, a certain amount of hydrolysis was observed for substrate 32b. The cause 
of this tendency could be due to the electron withdrawing effect of the chloride atom, which makes the 
carbonyl carbon more electrophilic, thus lowering the energy required for hydrolysis of the amide. However, 
if this was the case, the enzyme would properly show higher degree of deacylation towards the 
Figure 36 X-ray structure of HDAC2 co-complexed 
with the shown inhibitor, which reveal the 
ability of the enzyme to conserve moieties 
in the foot pocket. 
==PAGE:78==
64 
 
trifluoroacetylated substrate 34b. Therefore, it could be argued that this effect is caused by sterical 
interactions or a combination of those two factors. The chloride atom is by nature larger than a carbon atom, 
but it is smaller and spatial less demanding than a 
methyl group, which could give rise to this observed 
tendency. In addition, substrate 32b also showed 
slight activity towards members of the class IIa, 
including HDAC4, 5, and 7. The activity of these 
isoforms has not been shown by any other ε-N 
functionalized substrates than the trifluoroacetylated 
species, as previously mentioned. Therefore, for 
future development, it would be interesting to see if a 
substrate such as a mono- or di-fluoro acetylated 
substrate, could reveal a general and non-specific 
activity towards all of the HDAC isoforms.  
The activity of substrate 27b was very high for HDAC1, 2, and 3. This ε-N acrylamide derivative showed to be 
similar or even more active than the acetylated substrate, which made this modification ideal for an in-bead 
library, as will be discussed later. When this residue was added a methyl group to give the E-configuration 
(crotonyl, substrate 28b), a lot of the activity was lost, despite the fact that substrate 30b with similar length 
had shown deacetylase activity towards these three enzymes. This might be due to the more stiff acyl moiety, 
which impede its ability to reach the active site.  As mentioned in section 1.1 and 1.4, the two histone 
deacetylse enzymes SIRT1 and HDAC3 have shown to hydrolyse the ε-N crotonyl with minor effects (as also 
shown in this study) [42, 14]. The desire for revealing 
such activities is based on an extensive study, which 
revealed 28 sites on the core histones that were 
functionalized with crotonyl [127]. These PTMs were 
identified 
by 
mass 
spectrometry 
and 
HRMS 
determined a mass difference of 68.0230 Da (±0.01 
Da), which correspond to an element composition of 
C4H5O. From this observation, it was suggested that the crotonyl modification was the most likely to cause 
this effect. However, several other isomers with the same element composition could give the same mass, 
as shown in Figure 38. The presence of crotonyl on histones was confirmed by several follow-up experiments, 
including usage of pan anti-crotonyl antibody, which detected the presence of crotonyl on the core histone, 
and labelling with deuterium-labelled crotonyl-CoA, which subsequent was identified on 19 of the 28

positions. This thorough research clearly indicate the presence of crotonyl on the core histones. However, 
the lack of deacetylase activity of this modification in vitro towards any of the 18 different histone 
deacetylases, and the shown activity towards other acylation with a similar length or element composition, 
might indicate that these PTMs are recognized by other enzymes, or that they are precursors for further 
modification.    
Four of the screened compounds did not show any activity, including substrate 25b, 29b, 35b, and 36b. The 
loss of activity of substrate 25b is most presumably due to steric hindrance of the two bulky methyl groups 
on this moiety. However, similar functional groups as the benzoyl group on substrate 29b have proven to be 
able to reach the catalytic domain of HDAC1, 2, and 3 (Figure 36), although no enzymatic activity was 
observed. Jung et al. have observed similar tendencies with substrates containing an ε-N phenylacetyl amide 
[43]. The absence of activity for substrate 29b might be due to steric hindrance, but it could also be due to 
stabilization of the transition state by van der Waals forces between the benzoyl group and the lipophilic 
residues in the foot pocket, thus giving the compound an inhibitor effect.  
The two latter compounds, substrate 35b and 36b, both contain a primary amine and did not show any 
significant activity, despite having lengths corresponding to other active substrates. The reason for this could 
also be due to an inhibitor effect, where both the amine and carbonyl oxygen could potentially chelate to 
the active site Zink atom. However, no inhibitor effects of these three compounds have been investigated so 
far.  
Several of the screened ε-N acylations showed promising activity for an in-bead HTS library. Out of the 14 
tested acylations, five were chosen to be present in the OBOC library, including the propionyl, the acryl, the 
α-azide, the α-chloro, and the cyclopropyl, as shown in Figure 39. The three former mentioned acyl 
modifications were chosen due to their high activity and selectivity towards HDAC1, 2, and 3. Furthermore, 
the difference in activity between HDAC 1 and 2 towards the azide, made this modification very interesting 
to investigate further. The α-chloro moiety was 
chosen due its very distinct activity, which could be 
used to identify active substrates for the class IIa 
enzymes. Finally, the cyclopropyl was chosen due its 
high activity and selectivity towards HDAC3, which 
could be further improved with this in-bead setup. 
 
 
Figure 39 The five chosen ε-N acyl modifications for the in-
bead OBOC library.  
==PAGE:80==
