2.5 In-bead screening and development  
Upon the development of the OBOC library of approximately 63,000 different compounds, ideal in-bead 
screening conditions were required for being able to identify highly active substrates. The study of the HDAC 
isoforms activity towards different peptides fragment have been thoroughly investigated for the natural 
occurring amino acids, as mentioned in section 1.5. These studies have revealed the importance of the 
adjacent peptide sequences for the activity of the enzymes. However, several aspects of the screening setup 
needed to be verified and optimised before the in-bead screen could be performed. The first aspect to be 
considered was the length of the irradiation period. For this, a simple setup was carried out using beads 
functionalized with the deacetylated α-N Boc protected lysine (38a). The ε-N of the lysine was covalently 
attached to the photolinker, which insured that no on-bead hits would take place. The assay was carried out 
by partitioning the functionalized beads onto a polystyrene petri dish, followed by addition of HDAC buffer 
and irradiation of the beads for varying periods (see Figure 40). Immediately after the irradiations period, the 
Figure 40 Results from bead based dose-response experiments with varying irradiation periods. The upper part of the figure 
illustrates the procedure; A) Deacetylated beads were partitioned on a polystyrene petri dish. B) The beads were added 
HDAC buffer and irradiated for a given period. C) Developer (Trypsin) was added. D) The fluorescence was visualized 
under microscope. The lower part of the figure shows the brightfield and corresponding fluorescence images after a given 
irradiation period.  
==PAGE:81==
67 
 
beads were added the developer (Trypsin, 0.4 mg/mL), which ensured cleavage of the AMC-group from the 
photo released lysine substrates within the individual beads. Several irradiation periods were tested as 
shown in Figure 40. Initially it was found that when the irradiation was excluded, no fluorescence was 
observed. Then in different individual assays, the fluorescence intensity and ratios were visualized and 
examined with fluorescence microscopy (excitation at 360 and emission at 460 nm) at various irradiation 
periods. It can be seen from Figure 40 that unaffected of the length of the irradiation period, a non-
homogeneous fluorescence picture was observed. A slight increase in the ratio between beads that were 
“turned-on” and “turned-off” were found with increasing irradiation periods, ranging from 17% to 26% of 
beads showing desired fluorescence intensity. This was an unfortunate outcome and with activation of only 
a quarter of the irradiated beads, which showed varying fluorescence intensities, too many uncertainties 
were associated with this particular assay setup.   
As mentioned in section 1.7.3, this in-bead HTS technology rely on the preliminary studies, which revealed 
the utility of this method by identifying HDAC inhibitors with IC50 values in the nano molar range (data not 
published). The original procedure for identification of these inhibitors in an OBOC library containing 
approximately 60,000 compounds, was as follows: Beads containing potential inhibitors were embedded in 
HDAC buffer before being irradiated for 30 sec. The beads were then incubated with an aqueous solution of 
a tailored HDAC substrate for 30 min in the dark, which allowed the substrates to diffuse into the bead 
interior giving a homogeneous concentration of substrates in the beads, along with the photo-cleaved 
compounds. The beads were then gently washed with buffer to remove any excess of substrate from the 
solution, which could interfere with the fluorescence image. A solution of HeLa HDAC extract was then added 
to the beads, which hydrolysed the substrates, thus releasing deacetylated substrates, if no inhibition 
occurred inside the bead. If the enzymes had been inhibited, the substrates within that particular bead would 
not be deacetylated and recognized upon development by addition of Trypsin. 
Direct conversion of the inhibitor screening method proved to be difficult for this substrate identification 
assay for several reasons. First, when releasing inhibitors in a bead based assay, the concentration required 
to give a desired response is typically in the nano-molar range within one single bead, hence the short 
irradiation period. Whereas in a substrate assays, the concentration required for a desired fluorescence 
intensity lies in the micro-molar range. For that matter, irradiation periods required to photo-release this 
greater amount of substrate is needed and thus being more sensitive to the light scattering, shielding, and 
shadow effects associated with photo-release from solid support. Secondly, the homogeneity of substrate 
within a mixture of beads were obtained by solution-based diffusion, which cannot be accomplished in this 
setup. However, this diffusion of substrates into the bead interior additionally showed that the beads were 
==PAGE:82==
68 
 
capable of containing a sufficient amount of substrate to cause a desired response, without contaminating 
the surrounding solution.  
The previous presented experiments (Figure 40) indicated that the irradiation period was not the crucial 
factor for obtaining a uniform fluorescence image when photo-releasing active compounds from a heap of 
beads. Therefore, several different approaches were utilized as an attempt to increase the overall 
fluorescence homogeneity.  
The first hypothesis to be tested was how the clustering of the beads influenced the photo-release within 
the individual bead. If a bead is placed underneath a group of beads, the UV intensity reaching that particular 
bead would be different from the one being closer to the irradiation source. Therefore, an experiment was 
performed where the beads were irradiated for 30 min in HDAC buffer and then gently stirred each five 
minutes to ensure a more even irradiation of each bead. This however resulted in a higher degree of diffusion 
of the liberated compound from the beads to the aqueous media, and no fluorescence was observed (Figure 
41, A1-A2).   
In an aqueous environment, the polymeric beads have a tendency to stick more together, compared to when 
being in an organic environment. Therefore a series of experiments was carried out, where the beads were 
pre-swollen in an organic solvent prior to being spread onto the polystyrene petri dish. Any excess of solvent 
was then removed and the beads were irradiated for 20 minutes. This typically resulted in a more uniform 
layer of beads on the petri dish as desired. After photo-release, any remaining solvent was evaporated from 
Figure 41 Attempts to optimize the fluorescence homogeneity upon irradiation of the functionalized beads. Brightfield and 
fluorescence image respectively. (A1-A2) 30 min irradiation with gentle stirring each 5 min. (B1-B2) Beads pre-swollen in 
DMSO. (C1-C2) Beads pre-swollen in MeCN. (D1-D2) Beads pre-swollen in HFIP. (E1-E2) Cluster of fluorescent beads, 
which indicate diffusion of substrate among the beads in the cluster. Found from assay with beads pre-swollen in MeOH.
==PAGE:83==
69 
 
the beads under an infrared lamp for additional 10 minutes, to minimize the substrate diffusion upon washing 
and swelling the beads in the HDAC buffer. The solvents that were tested included, DMSO, MeOH, EtOH, 
MeCN, Dioxane, THF, and HFIP. Solvents with high boiling points such as DMSO and Dioxane showed poor 
results since all the solvent were not evaporated, thus promoting the diffusion of cleaved substrates (Figure 
41, B1-B2). Better results were obtained with the more volatile solvents, but these experiments still showed 
very poor fluorescence homogeneity (Best results were obtained with MeCN, Figure 41 C1-C2). The 
fluorescence intensity of the “active” beads was also lower than the corresponding beads from the dose-
response experiment. Using hexafluoroisopropanol (HFIP) revealed the best uniformity thus far (Figure 41 
D1-D2). The usage of HFIP as solvent did however also lead to red colorization of the beads, which indicate 
that the slight acidic conditions also promotes cleavage of the acid labile Rink amide linker.  Furthermore, 
another significant disadvantages of using pre-swollen resin beads in an organic solvent, was that these 
organic environments enabled the substrates to diffuse from one bead to another, when lying in a cluster of 
beads, as shown in Figure 41 (E1-E2).    
Despite a better spreading of the beads in a more uniform layer on the polystyrene petri dish, the general 
fluorescence image still revealed a large difference in the fluorescence intensity between the individual 
beads. The influence of the stacking effect was then verified by setting up an experiment, where only a few 
beads were added to the plate (15-30 beads). The beads were positioned on the petri dish without touching 
Figure 42 Further optimization of fluorescence homogeneity. (A1-A2) Irradiation of a few beads on 
polystyrene petri dish in HDAC buffer, with no influence of bead stacking (10X 
magnification, brightfield and fluorescence image respectively). (B) Beads after 
irradiation positioned on buffer surface. (C1-C2) Bead screening result when using the 
setup shown in B (10X magnification, brightfield and fluorescence image respectively). 
==PAGE:84==
70 
 
each other, hence all effects of stacking could be ruled out. Surprisingly, neither of these beads showed any 
sight of fluorescence, as shown in Figure 42 (A1-A2). This indicated that the beads that did not show any sight 
of fluorescence were presumably not due to any stacking or shielding effects. A reason for this could be that 
the polystyrene petri dish influenced the diffusion of the substrates from the beads to the surrounding media. 
The effect of the substrates being held inside an organic interior of a polymeric bead when being in an 
aqueous buffer, is a consequence of the difference in polarities of these two media. Therefore, the beads 
being positioned on (or sticked to) the surface of the polymeric petri dish, could lead the diffusion of the 
substrates within the beads towards the polystyrene surface of the dish and out in the surrounding media.  
To test this hypothesis, a setup was prepared that enabled irradiation of the functionalized beads in an 
aqueous environment without touching any polymeric material, as shown in Figure 42 (B). This was 
performed by adding in 850 μL buffer to a small glass vial and then positioning approximately 4 mg of beads 
on top of the buffer surface. The surface tension of the buffer prevented the beads from sinking to bottom 
of the vial. The beads were then spread out on the buffer surface by immersing the vial into an ultrasound 
bath. This was followed by irradiation of the beads lying on the buffer surface, transferring of the beads, and 
development by addition of Trypsin. The results from this screen were much more promising than any of the 
former performed methods, as shown in Figure 42 (C1-C2). However, approximately 33% of the beads did 
still not show a significant fluorescence intensity and further optimization are required for fully exploring the 
utility of this screening methodology.  
 
2.5.1 In-bead assay using HDAC1 
As mentioned above, the assay setup requires further development to enhance to homogeneity and intensity 
of the fluorescence in the individual bead. Especially the transferring of the beads from the buffer surface to 
the petri dish caused some uncertainties. However, as the deadline of the project was approaching, it was 
decided that the acylated substrates should be tested with the recombinant enzyme HDAC1. The reason for 
selecting this particular enzyme will be discussed in section 4.   
The setup was based on the optimized conditions, where the beads were spread out on the surface of the 
HDAC buffer in a small glass vial, followed by irradiation for 20 min. The beads were then transferred to a 
petri dish and any excess buffer was removed prior to addition of the recombinant enzyme 8 μg/mL. The 
enzyme concentration was based on the recommended concentration by supplier for solution based assays. 
==PAGE:85==
71 
 
After being incubated for 60 min at ambient temperature, the assay was developed by addition of Trypsin (4 
mg/mL).  
To test this enzymatic HDAC assay, two different setups were prepared with beads functionalized with known 
substrates. These two substrates included the Ac-Leu-Gly-Lys(Ac)-AMC (22b), which has previously shown 
great activity towards HDAC1 (see section 2.4), and Boc-
Lys(Tfa)-AMC (34a), which has not been tested in solution 
in this project, but is known for its low activity towards 
HDAC1 [42]. As shown in Figure 43, a clear difference in 
fluorescence intensity was observed between the ε-N 
acetylated substrate (A1-A4), and the trifluoroacetylated 
substrate (B1-B4). This verifies that this method can be 
used to distinguish between beads functionalised with 
different substrates, based on their fluorescence intensity.  
The assay was then finally tested on the OBOC library, as 
shown in Figure 44. PEGA resin beads have previously been 
stated to contain approximately 1000 beads/mg [138], 
which means that when carrying out an assay using 4 mg 
of beads, the ideal amount of substrates that are tested

are 4000 different substrates in one screen. As shown in Figure 44, fluorescence was detected from this assay 
in some beads, indicating beads containing active substrates were present in the OBOC library mixture. 
However, due to the uncertainty of ranging fluorescence intensity among the individual beads and the 
deadline of the project, no beads were taken out for further analysis.  
 
 
==PAGE:87==
73 
 
3 Conclusion  
 
This master’s thesis has sought to prove the concept of, and develop an in-bead high throughput screening 
platform to identify highly active and selective substrates for the Zink dependent HDAC enzymes. To establish 
such a screening technology, development and optimization of several steps were required. Initially a linker 
construct was developed, which allowed photo-release of lysine derivatives. The ε-N of a Boc-Lys-AMC 
construct was covalently attached to a Holmes photolinker through a reductive alkylation, where upon the 
resulting construct was coupled to the solid support. A significant feature of this linker construct is that it 
enables selective modification of both the α-N and the ε-N of the lysine residue, thus creating ideal conditions 
for a fast and efficient library development. In addition, since the effect of the acetylated ε-N of the lysine 
was masked by attachment to the Holmes linker, only photo-released substrates were recognized by the 
HDAC enzymes, hence no undesired on-bead recognition took place.  
To develop a reliable library with modifications on both the α-N and the ε-N part of the lysine, some pre-
solution based studies was performed. The information of HDAC subtype activity and selectivity towards 
substrates with varying acylations are limited. Therefore, a panel of 15 different substrates including 14 
different acylations were prepared and tested for their activity towards each of the 11 different Zink-
dependent HDAC enzymes. These studies did not only reveal a handful of novel and active modifications, 
which were ideal for the in-bead OBOC library, but also provided information that expanded the profiling of 
the individual subtypes and confirmed the previously discovered tendencies. This screen led to the discovery 
of the novel substrate 26b, which exhibited high selectivity towards HDAC3-NCoR1. 
Using split-and-mix synthesis, a OBOC library was developed forming di-, tri- and tetrapeptides containing 
both natural and unnatural amino acids. The ε-N of the lysine construct was modified with five different 
acylations and with α-N peptide elongation using 23 different amino acids, a library of more than 63,000 
different compounds was generated.  
To be able to perform a reliable screen and identify highly active substrates, the in-bead assay setup needed 
to be optimized. The surrounding media during photolytic cleavage proved to be a crucial factor, which 
influenced the diffusion of the liberated substrates upon cleavage from the solid support. It was found that 
when the beads were dispersed on the surface of the HDAC buffer upon irradiation, more uniform and 
intense fluorescence images were obtained. This setup was then used on beads containing two different 
substrates with different activity towards the recombinant HDAC1. These two assays verified that the in-bead 
HTS technology can be used to differentiate between photo-cleaved substrates with different activity, based 
on their respective fluorescence intensity. Additionally, the assay was also performed on the OBOC library, 
==PAGE:88==
74 
 
which revealed active substrates among the thousands of synthesized compounds, which hopefully in future 
development can be used to identify highly active substrates.  
 
 
==PAGE:89==
75 
 
4 Future work and development  
 
Designing and developing a new bead-based screening method is a time-consuming process, which require 
optimization of several steps before it can be utilized to identify highly active substrates. Figure 45 is slightly 
modified from Figure 24, which was presented in section 2, and it depicts the progress of the present project 
and the stages that this project enabled it to reach.  
 
To fully explore the utility of this method, further development would first require optimization of the in-
bead assay. So far, the optimized procedure for the assay exhibit fluorescence in approximately two-thirds 
of the irradiated beads. By fully understanding what causes the diffusion of the liberated substrate from the 
beads, more homogeneous fluorescence images can hopefully be achieved. This will not only minimize the 
uncertainties of the “hit” identification when screening the OBOC library, but it is also essential for 
identification of selective substrates, as will be discussed later. Upon full development of the in-bead library, 
the active “hit”-bead need to be isolated from the mixture, cleaved from the solid support, analysed by 
MS/MS to identify the peptide sequence, resynthesized, and tested in solution to verify its activity. This final 
process is somewhat more straightforward than the former presented in Figure 45. However, the analysis of 
the peptide sequence is typically a troubling process, which is not always fully addressed due to low 
compound quantities and ionization intensity of the degraded fragments.   
As discussed in this thesis, selective substrates and inhibitors are highly desirable in the pharmaceutical 
industry. The side effects associated with treatment using pan-selective HDAC inhibitors includes 
gastrointestinal disturbances, fatigue, and QT prolongation, hence the desire for creating selective  
Figure 45 Development process of a new in-bead HTS platform for identifying novel and highly active substrates. The 
process is divided into four main steps. The two first steps, including the linker development and the library 
development have been conducted and optimized in this project period. The in-bead assay is currently under 
development, and require further optimization before the final step can be conquered. 
==PAGE:90==
76 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and less toxic HDACIs. The idea of using this screening methodology to identify selective substrates emerged 
from the basis that only a minor percentage of the compound that is coupled to the solid support would be 
released upon photolytic cleavage. Therefore, the active beads from one assay could be isolated from the 
mixture, followed by a wash with an organic solvent, which would remove the liberated substrates, and then 
used in another assay. This concept is illustrated in Figure 46 above.

To carry out a selectivity setup as the one that is presented in Figure 46, a homogeneous substrate release is 
essential, so that no false negatives are found. As discussed in section 2.4, several of the ε-N modifications 
showed selectivity towards HDAC1, 2, and 3, including propionyl (23b), the acryl (27b), and α-azide (33b) (see 
Figure 34), which was used for the OBOC library in this project. Therefore, since this subclass selectivity has 
been revealed in the pre-solution based studies, a selectivity screen on these three enzymes is required to 
achieve subtype selectivity.  
To verify and optimize the selectivity screen, a mixture of two compounds with a known activity towards two 
different HDAC isoforms was required. The acetylated substrate 22b, and trifluoroacetylated substrate 34a, 
are known to possess such activity towards HDAC1 and HDAC8. Figure 47 shows enzyme-loading experiments 
for four different solution based assays using the two former mentioned substrates. These assays were 
performed to confirm the relative difference in activity and to identify the linear range for enzyme loading 
using 10 μM substrate concentrations. As shown, the two enzyme isoforms show a clear difference in activity 
towards the two different substrates, thus creating ideal conditions for the setup shown in Figure 48.  
It is my hope that in future delevopment by utilizing the selectivity setup, we will be able to identify selective 
substrates for the individual HDAC isoforms, thus expanding the profiling of these enzymes, which can be 
used to delevop new, potent, and selctive inhibitors.  
 
 
Figure 48 Illustration of a simple selectivity setup. Beads functionalized with two different substrates (22b and 34a) are mixed and 
liberated by photolytic release. An assay is then performed using recombinant HDAC1, and the active beads are isolated 
from the mixture. The negative beads are then wash with an organic solvent followed by an assay work-up using 
recombinant HDAC8. The negative beads from the first screen should now reveal a fluorescent dye. The active beads 
from each of the in-bead assays are then analysed to verify the structure of the substrate positioned on the corresponding 
bead.

5 Experimental Section  
 
5.1 General methods 
Unless otherwise stated, all solution based reactions were run under argon atmosphere and the glassware 
was dried over a Bunsen flame under vacuum. All solid supported reactions were performed on PEGA resin 
with a loading of 0.40 mmol/g (150-300 μm) and the solid-phase synthesis was carried out using plastic-
syringe techniques. The Flat-bottomed PE-syringes were fitted with PPE filters, Teflon® tubing and valves, 
which allowed suction to be applied to the syringes. TLC and LCMS was used to monitor the reactions and all 
new compounds were characterized by 1H-NMR, 13C-NMR, g-COSY, HSQC, HMBC, and HRMS. When sufficient 
quantities were available IR and melting point were moreover performed. All solvents were of HPLC quality 
and all commercial available reagents were used without further purification. Anhydrous solvents were 
obtained from the Innovative Puresolv system. Evaporation of solvents was performed using a rotary 
evaporator under reduced pressure at various temperatures. Analytical TLC was conducted using Merck 
aluminium sheets covered with silica (C-60 F254) and the plates were either visualized under UV-light or 
stained by dipping in a developing agent, followed by heating. Flash column chromatography was performed 
using glass columns with varying widths, which were packed with Geduran® Si 60 silica gel (40-63 μ) as 
stationary phase. For the recording of 1H-NMR, 13C-NMR, g-COSY, HSQC, and HMBC the Bruker Ascend 400 
(operating at 400 MHz for proton and 101 MHz for carbon) was used. The chemical shifts (δ) are reported in 
parts per million (ppm) and the coupling constants (J) in Hz. CDCl3 or DMSO-d6 were used as solvent. IR 
analysis was performed on a Bruker Alpha FT-IR spectrometer and reported in frequency of absorption (cm-
1). LCMS analysis was performed on a Waters AQUITY UPLC system equipped with a PDA and SQD 
electrospray MS detector using a AQUITY UPLC BEH C-18 column (1.7 μm, 2.1 x 50 mm. Column temp: 65oC. 
Flowrate 0.6 ml/min). Eluents; A (0.1% HCO2H in H2O) and B (0.1% HCO2H in MeCN) were used in a linear 
gradient (5% B to 100% B) in a total runtime of 2.6 min. HPLC analysis were performed on a Waters Alliance 
2695 HPLC system using Symmetry® C-18 coloumn 3.5 μm, 4.6 x 75 mm, column temp: 25 oC, (1 ml/min) 
with detection at 215 nm and 254 nm using diode array detector. Eluent A (0.1%TFA in H2O) and eluent B 
(0.1% TFA in MeCN) was used with linear gradient (100% A to 100 % B) with a runtime of 13 min. 
 
 
==PAGE:94==

5.3 General procedures for solid phase synthesis 
PEGA Resin preparation 
The PEGA resin was stored at 5 oC and swelled in EtOH. Before usage, the resin was washed in the following 
order with MeOH, H2O, DMF, MeOH, and CH2Cl2, six times of each solvent. The resin was then dried and 
stored at house vacuum. 
 
TBTU Coupling [143] 
NEM (4 equiv.) was added to a mixture of an acid (3 equiv.) and TBTU (2.88 equiv.) in DMF. The mixture was 
pre.-activated for 5 min. before being added to the beads, which were pre-swelled in DMF. The mixture was 
shaken for 2 h at rt., followed by washing with 6 X CH2Cl2 and 6 X DMF. If the subsequent coupling was a 
MSNT coupling, the resin beads was washed additionally with 3 X CH2Cl2 and one time with dry CH2Cl2. 
 
MSNT Coupling [144] 
MeIm (2.25 equiv.) was added to a suspension of an acid (3 equiv.) in dry CH2Cl2. Upon dissolution of the acid, 
MSNT (3 equiv.) was added to the mixture. The solution was then added to the pre-swelled resin. The reaction 
was proceeded for 1 h and was then washed with 6 X DMF, 5 X CH2Cl2 and one time with dry CH2Cl2. The 
procedure was then repeated one more time, to ensure full ester formation. The resin was washed with 6 X 
CH2Cl2 and 6 X DMF.  
 
HATU coupling [99] 
General procedure A; for split-and-mix library: DIPEA (4 equiv.) was added to a mixture of a Fmoc-protected 
acid (3 equiv.) and HATU (2.88 eq) in DMF. The mixture was shaken for 5 min before being added to the pre-
swelled resin. The reaction was proceeded for 1 h, before it was washed with 3 X CH2Cl2 and 3 X DMF. The 
procedure was repeated one more time, to ensure full aminde coupling. After the second coupling, the resin 
beads was washed with 6 X DMF, 3 X MeOH, and 6 X CH2Cl2.  
General procedure B; for coupling of the photolabile-lysine-derivative: DIPEA (3 equiv.) was added to a 
mixture of the phololabile-lysine-acid (20) (1.1 equiv.) and HATU (1 equiv.) in DMF. The mixture was shaken 
for 2 min. before being added to the pre-swelled resin. The reaction was proceeded for 3 h, before it was 
washed with 6 X DMF, 3 X MeOH, and 6 X CH2Cl2.  
 
==PAGE:117==
103 
 
N-Acetylation of primary amines [145] 
DIPEA (2 equiv.) was added to a solution of malonic acid (1.3 equiv.) and TBTU (1.6 equiv.) in dry DMF at 0 
oC. The mixture was stirred at these conditions for 10 min. before being added to a pre-swollen resin. The 
reaction was allowed to reach rt. and was shaken for 2 h before being washed with 6 X DMF, 3 X MeOH, and 
6 X CH2Cl2.  
 
Cleavage of HMBA linker for control-test 
70 μL of 0.1 M NaOH was added to 2-3 mg of functionalized resin. The mixture was then shaken for 10-30 
min. before 68 μL of 0.1 M HCl was added. The aqueous solution was then filtered using a filter pipette tip, 
and the remaining beads was additionally washed with approximately 70 μL MeCN.  
 
Cleavage of Rink-amide linker for control-test 
3-5 mg resin was added 50 μL TFA/CH2Cl2 (1:1, v/v) and shaken at these conditions for 10-30 min. The mixture 
was filtered using a filter pipette tip, and the remaining beads was additionally washed with approximately 
70 μL MeCN. The filtrate was further diluted with 800 μL MeCN, before the analysis was performed. 
 
Cleavage of Boc-protecting group 
General procedure A for resin containing a HMBA linker: A pre-swollen resin was added TFA/CH2Cl2 (17 μL/mg 
resin, 1:1, v/v) at rt. The mixture was shaken for 1 h before being washed with 6 X CH2Cl2, 3 X MeOH, and 6 
X DMF.  
General procedure B for resin containing a Rink-amide linker [146]: The resin was pre-swollen in 0.4 M 2,6-
lutidine in dry CH2Cl2 (5 μL/mg resin). In a separate vial was freshly prepared solutions of TMSOTf (0.4 M in 
dry CH2Cl2, 10 μL/mg resin) and 2,6-lutidine (0.8 M in dry CH2Cl2, 10 μL/mg resin) combined before being 
added to the resin. The mixture was shaken for 30 min. at rt before the solution was drained and washed 
two times with dry CH2Cl2. The deprotection procedure was repeated two times to ensure full removal of the 
Boc-group, and then washed with 6 X CH2Cl2, 3 X MeOH, and 6 X DMF. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:118==
104 
 
Cleavage of Fmoc-protecting group 
20% pip in DMF (17 μL/mg resin, vol%) was added to the resin. The mixture was shaken for 3 min before the 
solution was drained, and the deprotection procedure was proceeded for one additional time in 20 min. The 
resin was then washed with 6 X CH2Cl2, 3 X MeOH, and 6 X DMF. 
 
Cleavage of photolabile linker  
In a petri dish with a custom-made quartz cover was 5-250 mg of functionalized resin immersed in various 
solvents (10 μL/mg resin) and irradiated at 365 nm for 4-8 h. The beads was then filtered and washed with 
either MeCN or CH2Cl2. The crude products were isolated after removal of the solvent in vacuo.  
 
5.4 Enzymatic assays  
Materials for enzymatic assay 
All compounds that have been used for enzymatic assays was purified by preparative HPLC prior to use. The 
HDAC subtype comparative enzymatic activity assay was performed as a profiling service by SignalChem 
[147]. The materials that has been used for the assays included; HDAC1 obtained from Enzo Life Science 
(>70% purity by SDS-PAGE and CCB according to supplier), HDAC2 obtained from Enzo Life Science (70% 
purity by SDS-PAGE and CCB according to supplier), HDAC3-NCoR1 obtained from Enzo Life Science (>80% 
purity by SDS-PAGE and CCB according to supplier), HDAC4 obtained from Cayman Chemical (> 95% purity 
by SDS-PAGE and CCB according to supplier), HDAC5 obtained from SignalChem (> 90% purity by SDS-PAGE 
and CCB according to supplier), HDAC6 obtained from Enzo Life Science (>80% purity by SDS-PAGE and CCB 
according to supplier), HDAC7 obtained from Millipore (> 90% purity by SDS-PAGE and CCB according to 
supplier), HDAC8 obtained from Enzo Life Science (>90% purity by SDS-PAGE and CCB according to supplier), 
HDAC9 obtained from SignalChem (> 80% purity by SDS-PAGE and CCB according to supplier), HDAC10 
obtained from SignalChem (> 80% purity by SDS-PAGE and CCB according to supplier), and HDAC11 obtained 
from SignalChem (> 75% purity by SDS-PAGE and CCB according to supplier). HDAC assay buffer (50 mM 
Tris/Cl, pH 8.0, 137 mM NaCl, 2.7 mM KCl, 1 mM MgCl2, and 1 mg/mL BSA) was obtained from Enzo Life 
Science. Trypsin from bovine pancreas, obtained from Sigma-Aldrich (10,000 units/mg, TPCK treated with 
purity of 90-100%).  
 
 
 
==PAGE:119==
105 
 
Profiling Assay procedure 
The assays were performed in white non-binding CORNING 96-well assay plates, and were read using a 
Promega GloMax®-Multi Microplate Multimode Reader with excitation at 365 nm and detection emission at 
460 nm. With a final volume of 50 μL, HDAC assay buffer containing the substrate (20 μM giving a final volume 
of 10 μM) was assed to each well, followed by addition of the HDAC1-11 enzymes (25 μL of a 2 ng/μL solution, 
giving a 50 ng/ell final concentration), or HDAC buffer (25 μL) for control wells. The Plates were then 
incubated for 1 h at 37 oC before being added 50 μL trypsin (0.4 mg/mL). The assay were developed for 45 
min at ambient temperature, before being read. The adjusted activity for each target was determined by 
removing the blank control value. All assay points were performed in a duplicate.  
 
Enzyme concentration curve (linearity) 
The assay were performed in white non-binding CORNING 96-well assay plates in a final volume of 50 μL. A 
two-fold serial dilution of HDAC1 and 8 was conducted starting with a concentration of 10 μg/mL and 200 
μg/mL respectively, in a volume of 25 μL (giving a final concentration of 5 μg/mL) for HDAC1 and 100 μg/mL 
for HDAC8). Substrate 22b and 34a, were diluted in HDAC assay buffer from 20 mM stock solutions in DMSO, 
to give a final concentration of 20 μM. 25 μL of the HDAC buffer containing the substrate were then added 
to each well, and the plates were then incubated for 1h at ambient temperature before being added 50 μL 
trypsin (0.4 mg/mL). The assay were developed for 15 min, before being read, using a Tecan infinite 200 pro 
multiplate reader. The adjusted activity for each target was determined by removing the blank control value. 
All assay points were performed in a duplicate. 
 
Substrate 26b HDAC inhibition assays 
The assay were performed in white non-binding CORNING 96-well assay plates in a final volume of 50 μL. 
HDAC buffer containing substrate 26b were added to give a final volume of 10 μL in a two-fold serial dilution, 
starting from a concentration of 500 μM (giving a final max concentration of 100 μM). For HDAC1, 20 μL of 
commercial available deacetylase substrate (obtained from Enzo Life Science, Ac-Arg-His-Lys-Lys(Ac)-AMC) 
in a concentration of 37.5 μM (giving a final concentration of 15 μM, as recommended by the supplier) was 
added to each well. For HDAC8, 20 μL of commercial available deacetylase substrate (obtained from Enzo 
Life Science, Ac-Arg-His-Lys(Ac)-Lys(Ac)-AMC) in a concentration of 62.5 μM (giving a final concentration of 
25 μM, as recommended by the supplier) was added to each well. This was followed by addition of 20 μL 
==PAGE:120==
106 
 
HDAC enzyme (10 μg/mL for HDAC1, giving a final concentration of 4 μg/mL, and 125 μg/mL for HDAC8, giving 
a final concentration of 50 μg/mL). The plates were then incubated for 1h at ambient temperature before 
being added 50 μL trypsin (0.4 mg/mL). The assay were developed for 15 min, before being read using a 
Tecan infinite 200 pro multiplate reader. The adjusted activity for each target was determined by removing 
the blank control value. All assay points were performed in a duplicate. 
 
Bead-based assays  
In a small glass cylinder (1 cm in diameter) was added 850 μL HDAC buffer. 4 mg of the dried beads containing 
the active substrates were then carefully added on the water surface. Using ultrasound, the beads were 
spread out on the buffer surface, as shown in Figure 42. The beads were then irradiated for a period of time, 
before they were carefully transferred to a polystyrene petri dish. Any excess of HDAC buffer was removed 
from the beads, so that the beads only were covered by a thin layer of buffer. 150 μL of HDAC1 with a 
concentration of 8 μg/mL in HDAC buffer was added to the beads (If the deacetylated substrate was tested, 
this step was neglected), and were then incubated for 60 min at ambient temperature, before being added 
50 μL trypsin (0.4 mg/mL). After 15 min of development, the fluorescence of the individual bead was 
observed using fluorescence microscopy (excitation at 360-460 nm) on an Axiovert200M microscope, with a 
Deltapix 450P CCD camera at 2.5x and 10x magnification.

Appendix D - Project plan 
 
As a new requirement for the Master’s thesis on Applied Chemistry, this separate document is an audited 
version of the original project plan, which was handed to the supervisor one month into the course. The aim 
for this plan was to give an overview of the overall project with an estimated time-line for the workload that 
needed to be carried out.  
The aim for this project is to investigate the possibilities of the newly developed in-bead high throughput 
screening technology, thus reaching for substrates with high turnover and selectivity towards specific HDAC 
isoforms. A retro synthetic analysis of the compounds of interests is shown in Figure 1. As seen in the figure, 
the target substrate (1) is an AMC coupled lysine derivative, functionalized with varying amino acid sequences 
on the α-N and acyl-groups ε-N. Closely related substrates have previous been synthesized and tested by 
several research groups, which have revealed information regarding deacylation of lysine posttranslational 
modifications by specific isoforms, such as lysine decrotonylation by HDAC3. Furthermore, amino acid 
variations on both the C- and N-terminal site of an acetylated lysine have revealed affinity towards specific 
residues, such as basic amino acids (Arg, Lys) and hydrophobic/aromatic amino acids (Trp, Phe, Leu, Ile, Val, 
Gly, and Tyr).  
This project differ from the previous research by two main aspects. First, by exploring the advantages of the 
split-and-mix synthetic strategy, one can build a large library of compounds in a relatively short period. 
Combining this with the mild cleavage conditions of the photolabile linker (2), one can screen a large amount 
of beads containing different compounds in biocompatible conditions. Thus, exploring the phenomenon, that 
organic compounds have a tendency to stay localized inside the organic environment of a resin bead when 
the surrounding media constitute an aqueous buffer. Secondly, by using unnatural amino acids and acyl-
Figure 1  Retro synthesis of the target compounds. AA = amino acid, AMC = 7-amino-4-methylcoumarin, PG = protecting group 
==PAGE:215==
201 
 
groups with yet undiscovered electronic effects, this project seeks to investigate a large amount of new 
substrates, thus indicating selectivity towards certain isoforms and/or class’. 
The project can be divided into four main tasks, seen from a chemical perspective (task 2-3 and 5-6). The 
development of the photolabile linker was the first concern in the project, and as seen in the workplan (Figure 
2), it has proven to be a time consuming process. The coupling was proceeded between a known photolinker 
(5), and an α-N Boc-protected lysine derivative, functionalized with an AMC group (4). Both building blocks 
can be synthesised in 2-4 steps. The next step in the process is to validate the functionalities of the linker 
construct, by functionalizing the complex with several different acyl groups, and amino acids. Substrates with 
both novel and previously described acyl modifications shall be synthesized. This includes ε-N modified 
compounds, such as acetylated, trifluoroacetylated, and crotonylated, but also new compounds with yet 
undiscovered electronic properties. Among these compounds could be acyl-groups containing amines, 
alcohols, ethers, thioethers, halogens, azides etc. These types of acyl modifications could be interesting since 
the class IIa enzymes have shown to hydrolyse substrates with electron withdrawing functional groups.  
 
Upon formation and characterization of these substrates, the next task (task 4) is to screen them for biological 
activity, preferably against all of the eleven Zink-dependent isoforms. The purpose of this screen is to identify 
the activity of the enzymes and to guide the project towards which enzymes and acyl-groups that could be 
interesting to further investigate in an in-bead assay. Once the specific HDAC-isoforms have been selected,

Figure 2  Work plan for the project including the different tasks and their respectively time-line. Blue line indicates the estimated 
time-line one month into the project. Red line indicates the time that was spent on the individual tasks.  
==PAGE:216==
202 
 
the following task (task5) is to develop a library of approximately 56,000 different compounds (assuming 
usage of 7 different acyl-groups and different combinations of tetra-peptides using 20 different amino acids). 
The library is then ready to be investigated by using the in-bead HTS technology. The goal is to show that the 
active beads can be visualized upon cleavage of the fluorogenic AMC-group and selectively sought out in a 
mixture of beads. In addition, this method can be used to identify selective substrates by repositioning beads 
containing active compounds into another setup containing a different HDAC isoform. Finally, the beads 
containing the compounds of interests, should be analysed by a very sensitive analysis method, such as direct 
inlet QTOF MS/MS or MALDI-TOF MS/MS, resynthesized, characterized and screened in a solution based 
assay to verify the observed activity.  
 
Self-evaluation of the project 
Developing a new bead based screening method is a time consuming process and each of the tasks presented 
in Figure 2 required careful optimization. Continuing progress of this project was hampered by the obligation 
of each task to be fully developed and optimized before the subsequent tasks could be performed. For 
example, the synthesis of the target substrates could not be performed before the photolinker was 
synthesised and developed. This meant that when the time-line of a certain task was prolonged, it shifted 
the time-line for the subsequent tasks as well. This was especially crucial for the synthesis of the different ε-
N modifications. The low reactivity of this amine and the fact that no general acylation procedure could be 
obtained, resulted in an extended time-line for this task, as shown in Figure 2 (task 3). As a consequence of 
this, a more compressed working period for the remaining tasks was obtained. Especially task 6 (the in-bead 
development) will require more time for further development. However, this project has led to some 
interesting results, both regarding the development of the photolinker, but also the solution based screen of 
the 15 different substrates, which has given a good foundation for future development.
